Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
5 min
Defining Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Commentary defines tasawwur and tasdiq with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tasawwur and tasdiq and distinguish between conception and judgment in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is the raw mental image; tasdiq is that image plus a truth-claim.

This lesson unpacks the foundational division of knowledge into mere conception and conception-with-judgment. Understanding this distinction is crucial because logic itself is divided into the part that perfects conception (definitions) and the part that perfects assent (arguments).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'tasawwur' and 'tasdiq' in the Arabic. Notice that 'tasawwur' is defined first as 'mere conception', while 'tasdiq' is defined as 'conception with judgment'.
The Arabic terms are the same across the tradition.
2
Extract the definitions
From the source: 'العلم إما تصوّر فقط وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل' (knowledge is either mere conception, which is the obtaining of the form of a thing in the mind) and 'تصوّر معه حكم' (conception with a judgment). Write down the definitions in your own words.
Note that 'tasawwur' is not limited to simple concepts; the passage later lists complex types.
3
Test with examples
Consider the concept of 'prayer' (salat) – this is a tasawwur. Now consider the statement 'prayer is obligatory' – this is a tasdiq because it includes a judgment. Practice distinguishing between the two.
Use Islamic examples like 'fasting' vs 'fasting is prescribed'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بحث واحد ؛ وصدّر البحث بتقسيم العلم إلى التصور والتصديق لتوقف بيان الحاجة عليه.
فقال : العلم إما تصوّر فقط وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل وإمّا تصوّر معه حكم» وهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أوسلباً.
ويقال للمجموع: تصديق».
أقول : العلم إما تصوّر فقط) - أي تصوّر لاحكم معه - ويقال له
فلذلك أوردهما المصنّف في بحث واحد، وابتدء ببيان الحاجة، فشرع في تقسيم العلم إلى قسميه - أعني التصوّر والتصديق - لتوقفه عليه. فإن قلت : لاحاجة فيه إلى هذا التقسيم، بل يكفي أن يقال العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري – إلى آخر المقدمات. قلت : المقصود بيان الحاجة إلى علم المنطق بقسميه - أعني الموصل إلى التصور والموصل إلى التصديق - فلو لم يقسم العلم أولاً إلى التصور والتصديق ولم يبين أن في كل واحد منهما ضروريًاً ونظرياً يمكن اكتسابه من الضروري، لجاز أن تكون التصورات بأسرها مثلاً ضرورية، فلاحاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصور، وجاز أن تكون التصديقات بأسرها ضرورية، فلاحاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصديق، فلا يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً، وقد عرفت ان
المقصود ذلك (شريف). (۱) هذا التصور قديكون تصوّراً واحداً كتصور الإنسان، وقديكون متعدّداً بلانسبة كتصور الإنسان والكاتب، أو مع نسبة غير تامة أيضاً : إما تقييدية كالحيوان
الناطق، أو إضافية نحو غلام زيد، وإما نامة غير خبرية كقولك «اضرب»
In one discussion; and he began the discussion by dividing knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) because explaining the need depends on it.
He said: Knowledge is either mere conception, which is the obtaining of the form of a thing in the mind, or conception with a judgment, which is the attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively. The sum is called assent.
I say: Knowledge is either mere conception – that is, conception without judgment – and he called it... Therefore the author presented them in one discussion, and started with explaining the need, so he proceeded to divide knowledge into its two kinds – i.e., conception and assent – because it depends on it. If you say: There is no need for this division; rather it suffices to say knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired – and so on from the preliminaries. I say: The aim is to explain the need for the science of logic in its two parts – i.e., that which leads to conception and that which leads to assent. For if one did not first divide knowledge into conception and assent, and did not show that in each of them there is the necessary and the acquired that can be obtained from the necessary, then it would be possible that all conceptions, for example, are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to conception; and it would be possible that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to assent. Thus the need for both parts of logic together would not be established, and you have already known that this is the aim (Sharif). This conception may be a single conception, like the conception of 'human', or multiple without relation, like the conception of 'human' and 'writer', or with an incomplete relation: either restrictive like 'rational animal', or additive like 'Zayd's slave', or a complete non-predicative relation like your saying 'Strike!'
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological definition from a commentary on the Shamsiyya. It defines the two primary divisions of knowledge: tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). Tasawwur is the mere mental form of a thing without any judgment, while tasdiq is a conception accompanied by a judgment (positive or negative attribution). The commentary then defends why the author begins with this division rather than a simpler division like necessary/acquired. The objection is that one could just say knowledge is necessary or acquired. The answer is that the goal is to show the need for both parts of logic – one part leading to conception (e.g., definition) and the other to assent (e.g., syllogism). Without first dividing knowledge into these two categories, one might wrongly think all conceptions or all assents are necessary, making one part of logic unnecessary. So the division is essential. Finally, the passage lists types of tasawwur: simple (e.g., 'human'), multiple without relation (e.g., 'human and writer'), or with an incomplete relation (restrictive, additive, or complete non-predicative like a command). This clarifies that conception is not limited to simple ideas.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
حكم
إسناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between tasawwur and tasdiq?
A) Tasawwur is always simple; tasdiq is always complex
B) Tasawwur involves no judgment; tasdiq involves a judgment
C) Tasawwur is about things; tasdiq is about actions
D) Tasawwur is necessary; tasdiq is acquired

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into conception (mere mental representation) and assent (conception plus judgment), and both are needed for logic to fulfill its role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a tasawwur from the Quran (e.g., the concept of 'taqwa') and a tasdiq about it (e.g., 'Taqwa is the best provision'). Explain why each is classified as such using the definitions from the passage.
?
Identify whether each of the following is a tasawwur or tasdiq: (1) 'الجنة' (Paradise), (2) 'الجنة دار النعيم' (Paradise is the abode of bliss).

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
5 min
Why the Division is Necessary for Logic
Commentary defends the necessity of the tasawwur/tasdiq division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the argument that the division into tasawwur and tasdiq is necessary to show the need for both parts of logic.

Core Idea
Without dividing knowledge into conception and assent, you might think all knowledge is necessary.

The commentator defends why the author begins with this division instead of a simpler one. An imagined objector suggests that dividing knowledge into necessary and acquired is sufficient. The response reveals the purpose of logic: it has two parts, each perfecting a type of knowledge. The division into tasawwur and tasdiq is essential to demonstrate that both parts are needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objector's statement: 'لاحاجة فيه إلى هذا التقسيم، بل يكفي أن يقال العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري' (no need for this division; it suffices to say knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired). Understand why this seems plausible: it is a familiar classification.
The objector thinks the simpler division already shows the need for logic.
2
Extract the response
Read the commentator's reply: 'المقصود بيان الحاجة إلى علم المنطق بقسميه' (the aim is to explain the need for logic in its two parts). The response argues that without the tasawwur/tasdiq division, one might think all conceptions or all assents are necessary, making one part of logic dispensable.
The response uses a conditional scenario to highlight the necessity.
3
Understand the hypothetical
The commentator says: 'فلو لم يقسم العلم أولاً إلى التصور والتصديق ولم يبين أن في كل واحد منهما ضروريًاً ونظرياً... لجاز أن تكون التصورات بأسرها مثلاً ضرورية' (If one did not first divide knowledge into conception and assent and show that each has necessary and acquired parts, it would be possible that all conceptions are necessary, etc.). This shows the logical consequence.
The missing premise: logic is needed only for acquired knowledge.
4
See why the division is essential
The response concludes that without this division, 'فلا يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً' (the need for both parts of logic is not established). Thus, the division is necessary to prove that logic has two distinct functions.
This argument assumes that logic perfects both types of knowledge.

ويقال للمجموع: تصديق».
أقول : العلم إما تصوّر فقط) - أي تصوّر لاحكم معه - ويقال له

المقصود ذلك (شريف). (۱) هذا التصور قديكون تصوّراً واحداً كتصور الإنسان، وقديكون متعدّداً بلانسبة كتصور الإنسان والكاتب، أو مع نسبة غير تامة أيضاً : إما تقييدية كالحيوان
الناطق، أو إضافية نحو غلام زيد، وإما نامة غير خبرية كقولك «اضرب»
In one discussion; and he began the discussion by dividing knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) because explaining the need depends on it.
He said: Knowledge is either mere conception, which is the obtaining of the form of a thing in the mind, or conception with a judgment, which is the attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively. The sum is called assent.
I say: Knowledge is either mere conception – that is, conception without judgment – and he called it... Therefore the author presented them in one discussion, and started with explaining the need, so he proceeded to divide knowledge into its two kinds – i.e., conception and assent – because it depends on it. If you say: There is no need for this division; rather it suffices to say knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired – and so on from the preliminaries. I say: The aim is to explain the need for the science of logic in its two parts – i.e., that which leads to conception and that which leads to assent. For if one did not first divide knowledge into conception and assent, and did not show that in each of them there is the necessary and the acquired that can be obtained from the necessary, then it would be possible that all conceptions, for example, are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to conception; and it would be possible that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to assent. Tasawwur is the mere mental form of a thing without any judgment, while tasdiq is a conception accompanied by a judgment (positive or negative attribution). Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of not dividing knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq before discussing necessary and acquired?
A) It would be impossible to define any term
B) One might think all conceptions or all assents are necessary, making one part of logic unnecessary
C) The division into necessary and acquired would become invalid

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq is essential for establishing that both parts of logic (definitions and arguments) are needed, because each type of knowledge can be either necessary or acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the objector's and responder's arguments in your own words. Then, apply the same reasoning to a different science: imagine someone claims that in tafsir, only definitions are needed, not arguments. Use the commentary's logic to refute this.
?
Consider the following: In fiqh, some rulings are known necessarily (e.g., obligation of prayer) and some are acquired (e.g., details of contracts). How does the tasawwur/tasdiq division help establish the need for both definitions and analogical reasoning (qiyas)?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
8 min
Defining Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author defines tasawwur and tasdiq with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) by identifying the presence or absence of judgment (hukm).

Core Idea
Knowledge is either a bare concept or a concept with judgment.

In logic, all knowledge is divided into two fundamental types: mere concept and concept with judgment. Understanding this division is key to understanding how definitions and propositions work. The passage defines both terms and gives examples.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between concept and judgment
A concept is just the form of a thing in the mind; a judgment adds an affirmation or denial. The text defines tasawwur as 'acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind' and tasdiq as 'concept with judgment'.
2
Use the example of 'human'
The text gives 'human' as a concept without judgment, and 'human is a writer' as a concept with judgment. Note that the judgment can be affirmative or negative.
3
Apply the distinction to other cases
Try to classify: 'the world is created' (tasdiq) vs 'the world' alone (tasawwur). This illustrates how the presence of attribution (isnad) makes the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بحث واحد؛ وصدّر البحث بتقسيم العلم إلى التصور والتصديق لتوقف بيان الحاجة عليه. فقال: العلم إما تصوّر فقط وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل وإما تصوّر معه حكم، وهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً. ويقال للمجموع: تصديق. أقول: العلم إما تصوّر فقط - أي تصوّر لا حكم معه - ويقال له التصوّر الساذج كتصورنا الإنسان من غير حكم عليه بنفي أو إثبات. وإما تصوّر معه حكم. ويقال للمجموع «تصديق» كما إذا تصورنا الإنسان وحكمنا عليه بأنه كاتب أو ليس بكاتب. فلذلك أوردهما المصنف في بحث واحد، وابتدأ ببيان الحاجة، فشرع في تقسيم العلم إلى قسميه - أعني التصوّر والتصديق - لتوقفه عليه. فإن قلت: لا حاجة فيه إلى هذا التقسيم، بل يكفي أن يقال العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري – إلى آخر المقدمات. قلت: المقصود بيان الحاجة إلى علم المنطق بقسميه - أعني الموصل إلى التصور والموصل إلى التصديق - فلو لم يقسم العلم أولاً إلى التصور والتصديق ولم يبين أن في كل واحد منهما ضرورياً ونظرياً يمكن اكتسابه من الضروري، لجاز أن تكون التصورات بأسرها مثلاً ضرورية، فلا حاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصور، وجاز أن تكون التصديقات بأسرها ضرورية، فلا حاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصديق، فلا يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً، وقد عرفت أن المقصود ذلك. (شريف). أما التصوّر: فهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل، فليس معنى تصورنا الإنسان إلا أن ترتسم منه صورة في العقل بها يمتاز الإنسان عن غيره عند العقل، كما تثبت صورة الشيء في المرآة، إلا أن المرآة لا يثبت فيها إلا مثل المحسوسات والنفس مرآة ينطبع فيها مثل المعقولات والمحسوسات فقوله: وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل إشارة إلى تعريف مطلق التصوّر، دون التصور فقط؛ لأنه لما ذكر التصور فقط، فقد ذكر أمرين: أحدهما التصوّر المطلق - لأن المقيد إذا كان مذكوراً كان
In one section; and he prefaced the section with the division of knowledge into concept and assent because the explanation of the need depends on it. He said: Knowledge is either mere concept—which is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind—or concept accompanied by judgment, which is the attribution of one thing to another affirmatively or negatively. The totality is called assent. I say: Knowledge is either mere concept—i.e., a concept without judgment—called bare concept, like our concept of 'human' without any affirmative or negative judgment about it. Or it is a concept with judgment, and the totality is called 'assent,' as when we conceive 'human' and judge that he is a writer or not a writer. Therefore, the author presented them in one section and began by explaining the need, so he started by dividing knowledge into its two parts—namely, concept and assent—because the explanation of the need depends on it. If you say: There is no need for this division; rather, it suffices to say that knowledge is divided into necessary and theoretical—and so on for the rest of the premises. I reply: The intention is to show the need for the science of logic in both its parts—namely, that which leads to concept and that which leads to assent. For if he had not first divided knowledge into concept and assent and did not explain that in each of them there is something necessary and something theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, it would be possible, for example, that all concepts are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to concept, and it would be possible that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to assent. Thus, the need for both parts of logic together would not be established, and you have known that this is the intended purpose. (Sharif). As for concept: it is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind. So the meaning of our concept of 'human' is nothing but that a form of him imprints in the mind by which the human is distinguished from others in the mind, just as the form of a thing is established in a mirror, except that the mirror only establishes sensible likenesses, while the soul is a mirror in which are imprinted likenesses of intelligibles and sensibles. So his statement: 'It is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind' indicates the definition of absolute concept, not merely the concept (without judgment); because when he mentioned 'mere concept', he mentioned two things: one is absolute concept—because when the qualified is mentioned, the absolute is included
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent), a foundational classification in Islamic logic. The author begins by stating that the text's author (al-musannif) placed this division in a single section and prefaced it with the need for logic because the explanation of that need depends on it. He then defines knowledge: it is either mere concept (tasawwur only), which is the acquisition of a thing's form in the mind without any judgment, or concept with a judgment (tasdiq), which is an attribution of one thing to another, either affirmatively or negatively. The totality of concept and judgment is called tasdiq. The commentator elaborates: 'mere concept' is a concept without judgment, like conceiving 'human' without affirming or denying anything about it; 'concept with judgment' is like conceiving 'human' and then judging that he is a writer or not a writer. The commentator then explains why this division was necessary: if the author had not first divided knowledge into concept and assent, and then shown that both types can be either necessary or theoretical (i.e., acquired from the necessary), one might think that all concepts or all assents are necessary, thus eliminating the need for both parts of logic (one part leading to concepts, the other to assents). By making this division, the author demonstrates that both parts are necessary because each can contain theoretical elements that need to be acquired from the necessary. The commentator then defines absolute concept (tasawwur) as the acquisition of a thing's form in the mind, using the analogy of a mirror: just as a mirror receives the forms of sensible objects, the soul receives forms of both sensible and intelligible objects. He clarifies that the definition given is for absolute concept, not specifically for 'mere concept', because when the author mentioned 'mere concept', he implicitly included the absolute concept as one of its components.

Key Terms
تصوّر
تصديق
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes tasdiq according to the passage?
A) A concept without judgment
B) A concept with judgment
C) A judgment without a concept
D) The form of a thing in the mind

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is either a bare concept (tasawwur) or a concept with judgment (tasdiq); this division is foundational for logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as tasawwur or tasdiq: (a) 'I understand what 'riba' means', (b) 'Riba is forbidden in Islam'.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
10 min
Why Logic Needs Both Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author explains why the division is needed for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the division into tasawwur and tasdiq is necessary to establish the need for both parts of logic.

Core Idea
Without this division, one might think all concepts or all assents are self-evident, making logic unnecessary.

The commentator presents an objection: why not just divide knowledge into necessary and theoretical? The reply shows that without first dividing into concept and assent, we might assume that one of the two is entirely self-evident, thus eliminating the need for that part of logic. This section reveals the epistemological motivation for the division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector says: why not simply divide knowledge into necessary and theoretical? That would seem sufficient to show the need for logic. What is the point of the additional division into concept and assent?
2
Understand the commentator's reply
The commentator explains: if we don't show that both concepts and assents can be theoretical (i.e., non-evident), someone might think all concepts are necessary, so we don't need a method to acquire concepts; similarly for assents. Thus, the division into concept and assent is necessary to prove that both parts of logic (leading to concepts and leading to assents) are needed.
3
Restate the argument in your own words
Summarise: The goal is to justify both parts of logic. If all concepts were necessary, logic for concepts would be redundant. But some concepts are theoretical, so we need the part that teaches how to form definitions. The same for assents. The division into concept and assent allows us to make this point clearly.

في بحث واحد؛ وصدّر البحث بتقسيم العلم إلى التصور والتصديق لتوقف بيان الحاجة عليه. فقال: العلم إما تصوّر فقط وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل وإما تصوّر معه حكم، وهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً. ويقال للمجموع: تصديق. أقول: العلم إما تصوّر فقط - أي تصوّر لا حكم معه - ويقال له التصوّر الساذج كتصورنا الإنسان من غير حكم عليه بنفي أو إثبات. وإما تصوّر معه حكم. ويقال للمجموع «تصديق» كما إذا تصورنا الإنسان وحكمنا عليه بأنه كاتب أو ليس بكاتب. فلذلك أوردهما المصنف في بحث واحد، وابتدأ ببيان الحاجة، فشرع في تقسيم العلم إلى قسميه - أعني التصوّر والتصديق - لتوقفه عليه. فإن قلت: لا حاجة فيه إلى هذا التقسيم، بل يكفي أن يقال العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري – إلى آخر المقدمات. قلت: المقصود بيان الحاجة إلى علم المنطق بقسميه - أعني الموصل إلى التصور والموصل إلى التصديق - فلو لم يقسم العلم أولاً إلى التصور والتصديق ولم يبين أن في كل واحد منهما ضرورياً ونظرياً يمكن اكتسابه من الضروري، لجاز أن تكون التصورات بأسرها مثلاً ضرورية، فلا حاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصور، وجاز أن تكون التصديقات بأسرها ضرورية، فلا حاجة إذن إلى الموصل إلى التصديق، فلا يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً، وقد عرفت أن المقصود ذلك. (شريف). أما التصوّر: فهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل، فليس معنى تصورنا الإنسان إلا أن ترتسم منه صورة في العقل بها يمتاز الإنسان عن غيره عند العقل، كما تثبت صورة الشيء في المرآة، إلا أن المرآة لا يثبت فيها إلا مثل المحسوسات والنفس مرآة ينطبع فيها مثل المعقولات والمحسوسات فقوله: وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل إشارة إلى تعريف مطلق التصوّر، دون التصور فقط؛ لأنه لما ذكر التصور فقط، فقد ذكر أمرين: أحدهما التصوّر المطلق - لأن المقيد إذا كان مذكوراً كان
In one section; and he prefaced the section with the division of knowledge into concept and assent because the explanation of the need depends on it. He said: Knowledge is either mere concept—which is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind—or concept accompanied by judgment, which is the attribution of one thing to another affirmatively or negatively. The totality is called assent. I say: Knowledge is either mere concept—i.e., a concept without judgment—called bare concept, like our concept of 'human' without any affirmative or negative judgment about it. Or it is a concept with judgment, and the totality is called 'assent,' as when we conceive 'human' and judge that he is a writer or not a writer. Therefore, the author presented them in one section and began by explaining the need, so he started by dividing knowledge into its two parts—namely, concept and assent—because the explanation of the need depends on it. If you say: There is no need for this division; rather, it suffices to say that knowledge is divided into necessary and theoretical—and so on for the rest of the premises. I reply: The intention is to show the need for the science of logic in both its parts—namely, that which leads to concept and that which leads to assent. For if he had not first divided knowledge into concept and assent and did not explain that in each of them there is something necessary and something theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, it would be possible, for example, that all concepts are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to concept, and it would be possible that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for that which leads to assent. Thus, the need for both parts of logic together would not be established, and you have known that this is the intended purpose. (Sharif). As for concept: it is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind. So the meaning of our concept of 'human' is nothing but that a form of him imprints in the mind by which the human is distinguished from others in the mind, just as the form of a thing is established in a mirror, except that the mirror only establishes sensible likenesses, while the soul is a mirror in which are imprinted likenesses of intelligibles and sensibles. So his statement: 'It is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind' indicates the definition of absolute concept, not merely the concept (without judgment); because when he mentioned 'mere concept', he mentioned two things: one is absolute concept—because when the qualified is mentioned, the absolute is included
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent), a foundational classification in Islamic logic. The author begins by stating that the text's author (al-musannif) placed this division in a single section and prefaced it with the need for logic because the explanation of that need depends on it. He then defines knowledge: it is either mere concept (tasawwur only), which is the acquisition of a thing's form in the mind without any judgment, or concept with a judgment (tasdiq), which is an attribution of one thing to another, either affirmatively or negatively. The totality of concept and judgment is called tasdiq. The commentator elaborates: 'mere concept' is a concept without judgment, like conceiving 'human' without affirming or denying anything about it; 'concept with judgment' is like conceiving 'human' and then judging that he is a writer or not a writer. The commentator then explains why this division was necessary: if the author had not first divided knowledge into concept and assent, and then shown that both types can be either necessary or theoretical (i.e., acquired from the necessary), one might think that all concepts or all assents are necessary, thus eliminating the need for both parts of logic (one part leading to concepts, the other to assents). By making this division, the author demonstrates that both parts are necessary because each can contain theoretical elements that need to be acquired from the necessary. The commentator then defines absolute concept (tasawwur) as the acquisition of a thing's form in the mind, using the analogy of a mirror: just as a mirror receives the forms of sensible objects, the soul receives forms of both sensible and intelligible objects. He clarifies that the definition given is for absolute concept, not specifically for 'mere concept', because when the author mentioned 'mere concept', he implicitly included the absolute concept as one of its components.

Key Terms
تصوّر
تصديق
حاجة
مقدمات

A) All concepts would be theoretical.
B) The need for both parts of logic together would not be established.
C) Logic would be unnecessary altogether.
D) Assents would be considered a subset of concepts.

Key Takeaway
The division into tasawwur and tasdiq is essential to prove that both parts of logic (definitions and arguments) are needed, because each can contain non-evident elements.

The Mirror Analogy and Absolute Concept
Author elaborates on absolute concept using mirror analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of 'absolute concept' (tasawwur mutlaq) using the mirror analogy and differentiate it from 'mere concept' (tasawwur saadhij).

Core Idea
The soul is a mirror that receives forms; concept is the imprint of a form, not the judgment.

The commentator uses the analogy of a mirror to clarify how the mind acquires concepts. Just as a mirror receives forms without any judgment, the soul receives the forms of things, both sensible and intelligible. This analogy helps us understand what a concept is in its most basic sense: the mere presence of a form in the mind, before any assertion or denial.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the mirror analogy
The text says: 'the soul is a mirror in which are imprinted likenesses of intelligibles and sensibles'. Compare with a physical mirror that only captures sensible forms. The soul captures both sensible and intelligible forms, but the point is that the form itself is simply received, not judged.
2
Understand absolute concept (tasawwur mutlaq)
The passage states that the definition 'acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind' is the definition of absolute concept. Absolute concept is simply the form in the mind, regardless of whether it comes with judgment or not. It is the common element in both mere concept and concept with judgment.
3
Differentiate absolute concept from mere concept
Mere concept (tasawwur saadhij) is absolute concept plus the condition of no judgment. So absolute concept is broader. The text explains that when the author said 'mere concept', he implicitly included absolute concept because the qualified (mere concept) contains the absolute (concept) as part of its meaning.

The Pronoun Reference in the Definition of Tasawwur
Commentator resolves whether the definition of tasawwur refers to absolute conception or conception alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct referent of the pronoun in the definition of tasawwur and explain why it must be absolute conception.

Core Idea
The definition of conception must cover all knowledge, not just simple conception without judgment.

When the logician defines tasawwur as 'the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind', an ambiguity arises: does this definition apply to absolute conception (which includes judgment) or only to simple conception (opposed to judgment)? The commentator resolves this by showing that the definition must refer to absolute conception, otherwise it would exclude valid cases. This lesson walks through the logical reasoning that determines the correct referent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible referents
The pronoun in the definition can either refer to 'absolute conception' (mutlaq al-tasawwur) or 'conception only' (al-tasawwur faqat). These are the two senses of tasawwur distinguished in the division of knowledge.
Absolute conception is synonymous with knowledge (al-'ilm) and includes both simple conception and assent.
2
Test the first option: conception only
If the definition applied only to simple conception, it would exclude conception that is accompanied by judgment. But the meaning of 'attainment of a form of a thing in the mind' applies equally to both. Thus it cannot be exclusive.
The commentator states: 'It is not possible that it refers to conception only, because the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind applies to conception that has a judgment.'
3
Test the second option: absolute conception
Since the definition fits both types, it must cover the broader category: absolute conception. Therefore the pronoun refers to mutlaq al-tasawwur.
This means the definition is for conception in the broad sense, not the specific sense opposed to assent.
4
Understand the placement of the definition
The commentator explains why the definition is placed after the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq. The division is primary; the definition serves as a reminder that tasawwur has two meanings, and the broader one is being defined.
The definition of absolute conception alerts the student to the fact that 'tasawwur' can mean knowledge in general, not just simple conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلق مذكوراً بالضرورة - وثانيهما التصوّر فقط – أي الذي هو التصوّر الساذج - فذلك الضمير إما أن يعود إلى «مطلق التصور» أو إلى «التصور فقط »). لاجائز أن يعود إلى «التصوّر فقط »، لصدق حصول صورة الشيء في العقل على التصور الذي معه حكم فلوكان تعريفاً للتصور فقط لم يكن (۱) فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يعود إلى العلم ؟ قلت : فلا معنى لتوسيط تعريفه بين قسميه، بل ينبغي أن يقدم عليهما. فإن قلت : مطلق التصوّر مرادف للعلم - كما سيصرح به - فما الفائدة في الافتتاح بتقسيم العلم ثم بتعريف مرادفه الذي هو تعريفه في الحقيقة ؟ قلت : الفائدة في ذلك التنبيه على أن التقسيم هو العمدة في بيان الحاجة - دون تعريفه - لأنه معلوم بوجه ما، وذلك كاف في تقسيمه؛ أو التنبيه على أن تفسير العلم بذلك مشهور، ففسّر مطلق التصوربه ليعلم أنه مرادفه كما صرح بذلك في قوله : «تنبيهاً على أن التصور كما يطلق... الخ. فإن قلت : تقسيم العلم إلى تصوّر فقط و تصور معه حكم» يدلّ على أن معنى التصوّر أمر مشترك بين هذين القسمين، يتقيد تارة باقتران الحكم وتارة بعدم الحكم، فقد علم بذلك أن التصوّر يطلق على ما يرادف العلم ويعم التصديق، فلاحاجة في ذلك إلى أن يعرف مطلق التصور دون التصور فقط. وأما إطلاق التصور على ما يقابل التصديق، فذلك معلوم من المتعارف المشهور ولا مدخل فيه للتعريف - وهوظاهر - ولا للتقسيم، إذ لم يعلم منه إلا إطلاقه على المعنى المشترك، دون إطلاقه على خصوصية القسم الأول. قلت : الحال كما ذكرت لكن في التعريف تنبيه على ما يدل عليه التقسيم، إذ ربما يغفل عنه، ولهذا التنبيه فائدة ستظهر عن قريب (شريف). شرح المقدمة مانعاً لدخول غيره فيه؛ فتعين أن يعود الضمير إلى مطلق التصور - دون التصور فقط - فيكون حصول صورة الشيء في العقل» تعريفاً له. وإنما عرف مطلق التصوّر دون التصوّر فقط – مع أن المقام يقتضي تعريفه - تنبيهاً على أن التصوّر كما يطلق – فيما هو المشهور – على ما يقابل التصديق - أعني التصوّر الساذج - كذلك يطلق على ما يرادف العلم ويعمّ التصديق، وهو مطلق التصوّر.
The absolute mentioned necessarily - and the second is conception only, i.e., which is simple conception - so that pronoun either refers to 'absolute conception' or to 'conception only'. It is not possible that it refers to 'conception only', because the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind applies to conception that has a judgment; so if it were a definition of conception only, it would not (1) be a preventer of the entry of others into it. So it is determined that the pronoun refers to absolute conception, not to conception only, so 'the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind' is a definition of it. And they only defined absolute conception, not conception only - even though the context requires its definition - as a reminder that conception, as it is commonly used for what is opposed to assent, i.e., simple conception, likewise is used for what is synonymous with knowledge and includes assent, which is absolute conception. As for the judgment, it is the attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively. Affirmation is the positing of the relation, and negation is its removal. So when we say 'man is a writer' or 'is not a writer', we have attributed the writer to man and posited the relation of the existence of writing to him - which is affirmation - or removed the relation of the existence of writing from him - which is negation. So here one must first perceive 'man', then the concept of 'writer', then the relation of the existence of writing to man, then the occurrence of that relation or its non-occurrence. So the perception of man is the conception of the subject of judgment, and man as perceived is the subject; the perception of the writer is the conception of the predicate of judgment, so the writer as perceived is the predicate; and the perception of the relation of the existence of writing or its non-existence is the conception of the judgmental relation, and the perception of the occurrence (1) This includes categorical, conditional, and disjunctive judgments, positive or negative (Sharif). (2) The delay of perceiving the concept of 'writer' after perceiving 'man' - as required by the word 'then' - is not obligatory but is a matter of preference; it is better to observe the essence first then the concept of attributes; as for the perception of the relation of the existence of writing to man, it must be delayed until after both are perceived together (Sharif).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of tasawwur (conception) and the reason why it is placed as it is. The commentator begins by clarifying that the pronoun in the definition refers to 'absolute conception' (mutlaq al-tasawwur) and not 'conception only' (al-tasawwur faqat). This is because the definition 'attainment of the form of a thing in the mind' applies to any conception, including those accompanied by judgment (tasdiq). If it were only for conception opposed to judgment, it would incorrectly exclude conceptions with judgment. The commentator then addresses a dialectical objection: why is the definition of 'ilm (or tasawwur) placed after the division of 'ilm into tasawwur and tasdiq? The response explains that the division is the primary means of clarifying the need for 'ilm, while the definition serves as a reminder that 'tasawwur' has two senses: one synonymous with 'ilm (absolute) and one specific to conception without judgment (simple). The latter is well-known and does not need definition, but defining the absolute sense alerts the student to the broader usage. Finally, the passage concludes that the definition applies to absolute conception, and this is done to remind that tasawwur can mean both knowledge in general and the specific conception opposed to assent.

مطلق التصور

Definition of Hukm and its Types
Commentator defines hukm and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of hukm (judgment) and distinguish between affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb).

Core Idea
Hukm is attribution; it is either affirmative or negative.

The commentator begins with a precise definition of hukm as the attribution of one thing to another, and then splits it into two fundamental types: affirmation and negation. This lays the groundwork for understanding propositional structure in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of hukm
Find the sentence: 'الحكم هو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً'. This is the core definition.
2
Understand affirmation and negation
Affirmation (ijab) is positing the relation (iqa' al-nisba); negation (salb) is removing it (intiza'uha). These are the two modes of attribution.
Note that the relation itself is neutral; the judgment determines whether it is posited or removed.
3
Apply the definition to an example
Take the proposition 'Man is a writer' or 'Man is not a writer'. Identify the attribution of 'writer' to 'man' and whether it is affirmative or negative.
This shows how the definition covers both common types of propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحكم فهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً ، والإيجاب هو إيقاع النسبة، والسلب هو انتزاعها، فإذا قلنا : «الإنسان كاتب» أو : «ليس بكاتب» فقد أسندنا الكاتب إلى الإنسان وأوقعنا نسبة ثبوت الكتابة إليه - وهو الإيجاب - أورفعنا نسبة ثبوت الكتابة عنه - وهو السلب - فلابد هاهنا أن يدرك أوّلاً «الإنسان» ثم مفهوم الكاتب)، ثم نسبة ثبوت الكتابة إلى الإنسان، ثم وقوع تلك النسبة أو لا وقوعها ؛ فإدراك الإنسان هو تصوّر المحكوم عليه، والإنسان المتصور محكوم عليه ؛ وإدراك الكتاب هو تصور المحكوم به فالكاتب المتصور محكوم به ؛ وإدراك نسبة ثبوت الكتابة أو لاثبوتها هو تصوّر النسبة الحكمية، وإدراك وقوع (1) هذا يعمّ الحكم الحملي والاتصالي والانفصالي إيجاباً أوسلباً (شريف). (۲) تأخر إدراك مفهوم الكاتب عن إدراك الإنسان - كما تقتضيه لفظة «ثم»- ليس أمراً واجباً، بل هو أمر استحساني، فإن الأولى أن يلاحظ الذات أولا ثم مفهوم الصفات؛ و أما إدراك نسبة ثبوت الكتابة إلى الإنسان فلابد أن يتأخر عن إدراكهما معا (شريف).
As for the judgment, it is the attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively. Affirmation is the positing of the relation, and negation is its removal. So when we say 'man is a writer' or 'is not a writer', we have attributed the writer to man and posited the relation of the existence of writing to him - which is affirmation - or removed the relation of the existence of writing from him - which is negation. So here one must first perceive 'man', then the concept of 'writer', then the relation of the existence of writing to man, then the occurrence of that relation or its non-occurrence. So the perception of man is the conception of the subject of judgment, and man as perceived is the subject; the perception of the writer is the conception of the predicate of judgment, so the writer as perceived is the predicate; and the perception of the relation of the existence of writing or its non-existence is the conception of the judgmental relation, and the perception of the occurrence (1) This includes categorical, conditional, and disjunctive judgments, positive or negative (Sharif). (2) The delay of perceiving the concept of 'writer' after perceiving 'man' - as required by the word 'then' - is not obligatory but is a matter of preference; it is better to observe the essence first then the concept of attributes; as for the perception of the relation of the existence of writing to man, it must be delayed until after both are perceived together (Sharif).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines hukm (judgment) and explains the steps involved in forming an assent (tasdiq). The commentator states that judgment is the attribution (isnad) of one thing to another, either positively (ijab) or negatively (salb). Affirmation is described as 'positing the relation' (iqa' al-nisba), and negation as 'removing it' (intiza'iha). An example is given: 'Man is a writer' or 'Man is not a writer'. In these propositions, the writer is attributed to man. The commentator then outlines the necessary perceptions: first, the subject (man), then the predicate (writer), then the relation of existence of writing to man, and finally the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation. These perceptions correspond to the conception of the subject (al-mahkum 'alayh), the predicate (al-mahkum bih), and the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya). The commentator adds two footnotes: one clarifying that this definition covers categorical, conditional, and disjunctive judgments; the other noting that the order of perceiving the subject before the predicate is not logically necessary but is a preference, whereas the perception of the relation must be after both are perceived.

The Cognitive Steps in Forming Tasdiq
Commentator outlines the cognitive steps in forming a judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the four perceptions required for assent (tasdiq) and explain the preferred order of perceiving the subject, predicate, relation, and its occurrence.

Core Idea
Tasdiq requires perceiving the subject, predicate, relation, and its occurrence sequentially; the order of subject and predicate is preferred but not necessary.

After defining hukm, the commentator takes a closer look at the psychological process that leads to assent (tasdiq). He identifies four distinct perceptions that must occur, and two footnotes from Sharif clarify the logical necessity versus preference in their order. This exploration shows that forming a genuine proposition involves more than just combining words; it requires a structured cognitive act.

Guided Path
1
List the four things that must be perceived
From the passage: first, the subject ('man'); second, the predicate ('writer'); third, the relation of existence of writing to man; fourth, the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation.
2
Map each perception to a component of the proposition
Perception of the subject = conception of al-mahkum 'alayh; perception of the predicate = conception of al-mahkum bih; perception of the relation = conception of al-nisba al-hukmiyya; perception of occurrence = the actual judgment.
The first two are conceptual, the third is the relational concept, and the fourth is the assent itself.
3
Analyze the footnote on order
The word 'then' (ثم) might suggest subject must come before predicate, but the footnote clarifies this is only a preference (istihsani), not a logical necessity. It is better to conceive the essence first, then the attribute.
4
Examine the necessity of the relation after both
The second footnote states that the perception of the relation (nisba) must necessarily be delayed until after both the subject and predicate are perceived together. This is a logical requirement, not just preference.
This distinguishes the relation perception from the order of subject/predicate.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Distinguishing Conception (Tasawwur) from Judgment (Hukm)
Commentary clarifies tasawwur and hukm, distinguishing them from mere conception of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three cognitive stages in a proposition: conception of subject, conception of predicate, conception of the judgmental relation, and the act of judgment, and explain why judgment is distinct from mere conception.

Core Idea
Conception of a relation is not yet judgment; judgment is the act of affirming or denying that relation.

This passage from the Qutbi commentary clarifies a foundational distinction in Islamic epistemology: the difference between simply grasping concepts and relations (tasawwur) and actively assenting to or rejecting them (tasdiq/hukm). The commentator uses a simple Arabic sentence 'Man is a writer' to illustrate the mental steps. By breaking down the process, we can understand how knowledge is structured in logic and how doubt differs from certainty. Let's walk through the steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two mental acts
The passage speaks of tasawwur (conception) and hukm (judgment). First, note that tasawwur can be absolute (including both simple conception and assent) or specific (opposed to assent). The definition given is for absolute conception: 'attainment of the form of a thing in the mind'. Judgment is defined as attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively.
The commentator mentions both usages of tasawwur to avoid confusion.
2
Break down the example proposition
Take the sentence 'Man is a writer'. Identify the subject (man), predicate (writer), and the relation between them (the existence of writing). The passage says we must first perceive 'man', then 'the concept of writer', then the relation of writing to man, then the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation.
These are four perceptions. The first two are simple conceptions; the third is conception of the relation; the fourth is the judgment.
3
Distinguish the conception of the relation from the judgment
The passage explicitly states that one can perceive the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) without making a judgment, as in doubt or assumption. Judgment adds the element of affirming or denying the relation. Conception of the relation is necessary for judgment, but not sufficient.
This is the key insight: conception is a prerequisite, not the same as judgment.
4
Apply to a new example
Now consider a proposition from fiqh: 'Wine is forbidden (haram)'. Identify subject (wine), predicate (forbidden), relation (the existence of prohibition). A person who understands the terms and the relation but is not sure whether wine is forbidden has conceived the relation but not judged. When a jurist issues a fatwa that wine is forbidden, they are making a judgment.
This shows how the framework applies to real Islamic knowledge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح المقدمة مانعاً لدخول غيره فيه؛ فتعين أن يعود الضمير إلى مطلق التصور - دون التصور فقط - فيكون حصول صورة الشيء في العقل» تعريفاً له.
وإنما عرف مطلق التصوّر دون التصوّر فقط – مع أن المقام يقتضي تعريفه - تنبيهاً على أن التصوّر كما يطلق – فيما هو المشهور – على ما يقابل التصديق - أعني التصوّر الساذج - كذلك يطلق على ما يرادف العلم ويعمّ التصديق، وهو مطلق التصوّر.
وأما الحكم فهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً ، والإيجاب هو إيقاع النسبة، والسلب هو انتزاعها، فإذا قلنا : «الإنسان كاتب» أو : «ليس بكاتب» فقد أسندنا الكاتب إلى الإنسان وأوقعنا نسبة ثبوت الكتابة إليه - وهو الإيجاب - أورفعنا نسبة ثبوت الكتابة عنه - وهو السلب - فلابد هاهنا أن يدرك أوّلاً «الإنسان» ثم مفهوم الكاتب)، ثم نسبة ثبوت الكتابة إلى الإنسان، ثم وقوع تلك النسبة أو لا وقوعها ؛ فإدراك الإنسان هو تصوّر المحكوم عليه، والإنسان المتصور محكوم عليه ؛ وإدراك الكتاب هو تصور المحكوم به فالكاتب المتصور محكوم به ؛ وإدراك نسبة ثبوت الكتابة أو لاثبوتها هو تصوّر النسبة الحكمية، وإدراك وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها - بمعني إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أوليست بواقعة)- هو الحكم، وربما يحصل إدراك النسبة الحكمية بدون الحكم، كمن تشكك في النسبة أو توهمها، فإنّ الشك في النسبة أو توهمها بدون تصورها محال، لكن التصديق لا يحصل مالم يحصل الحكم.
Commentary on the Introduction: preventing the entry of other than it into it; so it is determined that the pronoun refers to absolute conception (mutlaq al-tasawwur) – not merely conception (al-tasawwur) – so 'the attainment of the form of a thing in the mind' is its definition. And he defined absolute conception rather than just conception – even though the context requires its definition – as a reminder that conception, as is well-known, is sometimes used for what is opposed to assent (tasdiq) – that is, bare conception – and likewise it is used for what is synonymous with knowledge and includes assent, and that is absolute conception. As for judgment (hukm), it is the attribution of one thing to another positively or negatively. Affirmation (ijab) is the positing of the relation, and negation (salib) is its removal. So when we say 'Man is a writer' or 'He is not a writer', we have attributed the writer to man and posited the relation of the existence of writing to him – which is affirmation – or we have removed the relation of the existence of writing from him – which is negation. Therefore, here one must first perceive 'man', then the concept of 'writer', then the relation of the existence of writing to man, then the occurrence of that relation or its non-occurrence. So the perception of man is the conception of the subject (al-mahkum 'alayhi), and the perceived man is the subject; the perception of writing is the conception of the predicate (al-mahkum bihi), and the perceived writer is the predicate; the perception of the relation of the existence of writing or its non-existence is the conception of the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya); and the perception of the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence – meaning the perception that the relation exists or does not exist – is the judgment. And perhaps the perception of the judgmental relation occurs without judgment, such as one who doubts the relation or assumes it, for doubt about the relation or assumption of it without conceiving it is impossible, but assent does not occur unless judgment occurs.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological commentary clarifying the definitions of conception (tasawwur) and judgment (hukm) as foundational concepts in Islamic logic. The commentator begins by explaining that the definition of conception ('attainment of the form of a thing in the mind') refers to absolute conception, which includes both bare conception and assent, contrary to the narrower usage where conception is opposed to assent. This is a key distinction: sometimes 'tasawwur' is used synonymously with 'knowledge' and covers both conception and assent, but in logic it often specifically means the simple apprehension of a concept without judgment. The commentator then defines judgment as an attribution (isnad) of one thing to another, either positively (ijab) or negatively (salb). He gives an Islamic example: 'Man is a writer' or 'Man is not a writer' – here the subject is 'man', the predicate 'writer', and the relation is the existence of writing. He outlines the necessary sequence of perceptions: first the subject, then the predicate, then the judgmental relation between them, and finally the act of judging (perceiving the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation). Importantly, he notes that one can conceive the judgmental relation without actually judging – for instance, a person who doubts the relation or assumes it in a weak sense has the conception of the relation but has not yet committed to a judgment. This shows that conception of the relation is distinct from the judgment itself. The passage thus builds a precise framework for understanding how knowledge is structured in Islamic epistemology, using logical-linguistic examples from ordinary Arabic statements.

Key Terms
التصور
الحكم
النسبة الحكمية

A) There is no difference; they are the same act.
B) Conception of the relation is possible without judgment, as in doubt or assumption.
C) Judgment occurs before conceiving the relation.
D) Conception of the relation requires judgment to be valid.

Key Takeaway
Conception of the judgmental relation is a necessary prerequisite for judgment but is distinct from the act of judgment itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
5 min
Judgment vs. Perception of Propositional Relation
Author defines judgment and contrasts it with relation perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya and hukm, and explain why a doubter has the former without the latter.

Core Idea
Judgment is definitive perception of truth; grasping the relation is only a precondition.

In Islamic logic, it is essential to separate the simple act of understanding a propositional relation from the act of affirming or denying its truth. This passage from the Qutbi tradition clarifies that distinction using the example of doubt, showing that judgment requires certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cognitive states
Locate the terms 'idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya' (perception of the propositional relation) and 'hukm' (judgment) in the Arabic. Notice that both are perceptions, but of different objects.
The passage explicitly contrasts them.
2
Understand the definition of judgment
The author defines hukm as 'the perception that the relation is actual or not actual.' He clarifies that this is not merely conceiving 'occurrence' as an attribute of the relation, but perceiving the truth value.
Affirmative judgment: perception that the relation holds. Negative judgment: perception that it does not hold.
3
Analyze the doubt example
The doubter has perceived the propositional relation (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') but is uncertain about its truth. Hence he has idrak al-nisba but not hukm. This proves they are different.
Assent (tasdiq) only occurs with hukm.
4
Recognize the logical priority
The passage states that perception of the relation must precede judgment, just as perception of the terms precedes the relation. This establishes a necessary order in cognition.
This order is critical for understanding how knowledge is built.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة أو لا وقوعها - بمعني إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أوليست بواقعة)- هو الحكم، وربما يحصل إدراك النسبة الحكمية بدون الحكم، كمن تشكك في النسبة أو توهمها، فإنّ الشك في النسبة أو توهمها بدون تصورها محال، لكن التصديق لا يحصل مالم يحصل الحكم. (1) يريد به أنا لانعني بإدراك وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها أن يدرك معنى الوقوع أو اللاوقوع مضافاً إلى النسبة، فإن إدراكهما بهذا المعنى ليس حكماً - بل هو إدراك مركب تقييدي من قبيل الإضافة - بل نعني بإدراك الوقوع» أن يدرك أن النسبة ،واقعة، ويسمى هذا الإدراك حكماً إيجابيا، وبـ «إدراك عدم الوقوع» أن يدرك أن النسبة ليست بواقعة، ويسمّى هذا الإدراك حكماً سلبياً ؛ ولاشك أن إدراك وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها يجب أن يتأخر عن إدراك النسبة الحكمية، كما يجب تأخر إدراكها عن إدراك طرفيها (شريف). (٢) لاخفاء في تمايز إدراك «الإنسان» وإدراك مفهوم «الكاتب» وإدراك النسبة بينهما، وإنما الالتباس بين إدراك النسبة الحكمية وبين الإدراك الذي سميناه «حكماً» فلذلك أشار إلى تمايزهما فقال: «ربما يحصل إدراك النسبة الحكمية بدون الحكم» فإن المتشكك في النسبة الحكميّة متردّد بين وقوعها أو لا وقوعها، فقد حصل له إدراك النسبة الحكمية قطعاً، ولم يحصل له الإدراك المسمّى بالحكم، فهما متغايران جزماً. وكذلك من ظن وقوع النسبة وتوهم عدم وقوعها، فإنه قد حصل له إدراك النسبة الحكميّة وتجويز جانب السلب - تجويزاً مرجوحاً - ولم يحصل له الحكم السلبي، فإدراك النسبة الحكمية مغاير للحكم السلبي، وإذا ظن عدم وقوعها وتوهم وقوعها، فقد حصل له إدراك النسبة الحكمية وتجويز جانب الإيجاب - تجويزاً مرجوحاً - ولم يحصل له الحكم الإيجابي. فإدراك النسبة الحكمية مغاير للحكم الإيجابي أيضاً (شريف).
Judgment is the perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation — meaning the perception that the relation is actual or not actual. And sometimes the perception of the propositional relation occurs without judgment, like one who doubts the relation or assumes it. For doubt about the relation or assumption of it without conceiving it is impossible, but assent does not occur unless judgment occurs. (1) He intends by this: by 'perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation' we do not mean that one perceives the meaning of occurrence or non-occurrence as added to the relation; for such perception is not judgment — rather it is a compound restrictive perception of the type of addition. Rather, by 'perception of occurrence' we mean that one perceives that the relation is actual; this perception is called affirmative judgment. By 'perception of non-occurrence' we mean that one perceives that the relation is not actual; this perception is called negative judgment. There is no doubt that the perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation must be subsequent to the perception of the propositional relation, just as the perception of the propositional relation must be subsequent to the perception of its two terms (Sharif). (2) There is no obscurity in the distinction between the perception of 'man', the perception of the concept 'writer', and the perception of the relation between them. Rather, the confusion is between the perception of the propositional relation and the perception that we called 'judgment'. Therefore he pointed to their distinction saying: 'Sometimes the perception of the propositional relation occurs without judgment.' For the doubter concerning the propositional relation is hesitant between its occurrence or non-occurrence; he has definitely obtained the perception of the propositional relation, but has not obtained the perception called judgment. Thus they are certainly divergent. Similarly, one who assumes the occurrence of the relation and imagines its non-occurrence: he has obtained the perception of the propositional relation and an inclination toward the side of negation — a weak inclination — but he has not obtained the negative judgment. So the perception of the propositional relation is different from the negative judgment. And if he assumes non-occurrence and imagines occurrence, he has obtained the perception of the propositional relation and an inclination toward affirmation — a weak inclination — but he has not obtained the affirmative judgment. Thus the perception of the propositional relation is also different from the affirmative judgment (Sharif).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of judgment (hukm) and carefully distinguishes it from the mere perception of a propositional relation (idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya). The author begins by quoting the original text: judgment is the perception that a relation is actual or not actual. He then clarifies that this does not mean perceiving the abstract meanings of 'occurrence' or 'non-occurrence' as separate entities added to the relation. Rather, it means directly perceiving that the relation holds (affirmative judgment) or does not hold (negative judgment). The perception of the propositional relation must logically precede the judgment, just as the perception of the terms must precede the relation. To illustrate the distinction, the author gives the example of a person who doubts the relation. The doubter has clearly perceived the propositional relation (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') but does not have judgment because he is uncertain whether it is true. Similarly, someone who assumes the relation to be true while imagining it false has the perception of the relation but lacks the definite judgment. This shows that the perception of the propositional relation is separate from judgment, whether affirmative or negative. The passage uses the technical terms 'idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya' (perception of the propositional relation) and 'hukm' (judgment) and establishes that assent (tasdiq) requires judgment, not just the perception of the relation.

إدراك النسبة الحكمية

B) Hukm includes a definitive assessment of truth or falsity, while idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya does not.
C) Idrak al-nisba al-hukmiyya occurs after judgment, not before.

Key Takeaway
Perception of a propositional relation is a precondition for judgment, but judgment adds the element of truth assessment, which is necessary for assent (tasdiq).

Debate on Whether Judgment Is an Act or Perception
Author refutes the later logicians' act view of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the act view and perception view of judgment (hukm) and articulate the introspective argument that shows judgment is a perception.

Core Idea
Judgment is not an act of the soul — it is a passive perception of truth correspondence.

In this passage, the author critically examines a shift in Islamic logic: later logicians claimed that judgment (hukm) is an act of the soul, not a perception. By introspective analysis, he refutes this and reaffirms the perception view. Understanding this debate clarifies the nature of assent (tasdiq) and its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing views
Later logicians claim judgment is an act (fi'l); the author says it is a perception (idrak). Locate both positions in the text.
The later logicians' position is introduced at the beginning; the author's refutation follows.
2
Analyse the linguistic argument
The later logicians based their view on active verbs like 'affirm' and 'negate'. The author rejects this linguistic inference. Note why he considers it misleading.
The passage states that the truth is determined by introspection, not linguistic expressions.
3
Perform introspective verification
Follow the author's instruction: after perceiving a propositional relation (e.g., 'All causes have effects'), do you experience an act or a passive recognition of truth?
The author claims you only find a perception of correspondence or non-correspondence.
4
Examine the definitions of perception
Two interpretations of idrak are given: the soul being imprinted with a form (an impression) or the form itself (a quality). Show that neither qualifies as an act.
Both interpretations are incompatible with fi'l, which is about effecting change.
5
Summarise consequences for tasdiq
How does the view affect the definition of assent? If judgment is perception, tasdiq includes four perceptions; if not, three perceptions plus judgment.
The passage mentions al-Razi's and the philosophers' views on tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند متأخري المنطقيين) أن الحكم - أي إيقاع النسبة أو انتزاعها - فعل من أفعال النفس فلا يكون إدراكاً ، لأن الإدراك انفعال والفعل لا يكون انفعالاً (٢). فلو قلنا : «إنّ الحكم إدراك» يكون التصديق مجموع التصورات الأربعة، وهو تصوّر المحكوم عليه وتصوّر المحكوم به وتصور النسبة الحكميّة والتصوّر الذي هو الحكم. وإن قلنا : «إنه ليس بإدراك» يكون التصديق مجموع التصورات الثلاثة ،والحكم، هذا على رأي الإمام". وأما على رأي الحكماء فالتصديق هو الحكم فقط ، والفرق بينهما من وجوه : (۱) قدتو هموا أن الحكم فعل من أفعال النفس الصادرة عنها بناء على أن الألفاظ التي يعبر بها عن الحكم تدل على ذلك - كالإسناد و الإيقاع والانتزاع والإيجاب والسلب وغيرها - والحق أنه إدراك - لا فعل - لأنا إذا رجعنا إلى وجداننا علمنا أنه بعد إدراكنا النسبة الحكميّة الحمليّة أو الاتصالية أو الانفصالية لم يحصل لنا سوى إدراك أن تلك النسبة واقعة أي مطابقة لما في نفس الأمر، أو إدراك أنها ليست بواقعة أي غير مطابقة لما في نفس الأمر (شريف). (۲) ذلك لأن الفعل هو التأثير وإيجاد الأثر، والانفعال هو التأثر وقبول الأثر، فلا يصدق أحدهما على ما يصدق عليه الآخر بالضرورة، وأما أن الإدراك انفعال، يصح إذا فسر الإدراك بـ «انتقاش النفس بالصورة الحاصلة من الشيء» وأما إذا فسر بـ «الصورة الحاصلة في النفس فيكون من مقولة الكيف، فإنما فلا يكون فعلاً أيضاً (شريف).
And according to later logicians, judgment — i.e., affirming or negating the relation — is an act of the soul and thus is not a perception, because perception is an impression (infi'al) and an act is not an impression. So if we say 'judgment is a perception', then assent (tasdiq) is the sum of four perceptions: the perception of the subject, the perception of the predicate, the perception of the propositional relation, and the perception that is the judgment. But if we say 'it is not a perception', then assent is the sum of the three perceptions and the judgment. This is according to Imam (al-Razi). As for the view of the philosophers (hukama'), assent is only the judgment. The difference between them is in several aspects: (1) They falsely thought that judgment is an act of the soul emanating from it, based on the fact that the words used to express judgment indicate that — like 'predication', 'affirming', 'negating', 'affirmative', 'negative', and others — but the truth is that it is a perception, not an act. For if we return to our introspection, we know that after we perceive the propositional relation — whether categorical, conditional, or disjunctive — nothing occurs to us except the perception that that relation is actual, i.e., corresponding to reality, or the perception that it is not actual, i.e., not corresponding to reality (Sharif). (2) That is because act is the effecting and producing an effect, while impression is being affected and receiving an effect, so one does not apply to what the other applies to necessarily. As for the claim that perception is an impression, it is valid if perception is interpreted as 'the soul's being imprinted with the form obtained from the thing'. But if it is interpreted as 'the form obtained in the soul', then it belongs to the category of quality (kayf), and thus it is also not an act (Sharif).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a critical discussion of the nature of judgment (hukm). The author first states the view of later logicians (muta'akhkhirun) that judgment is an act (fi'l) of the soul, not a perception (idrak), because perception is an impression (infi'al) and act and impression are mutually exclusive. Based on this, the passage examines the consequences for the definition of assent (tasdiq). If judgment were a perception, then tasdiq would consist of four perceptions: the subject, the predicate, the propositional relation, and the judgment itself. If it is not a perception, then tasdiq consists of three perceptions plus the judgment as a separate act. This is the position of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. In contrast, the philosophers (hukama') hold that tasdiq is simply the judgment alone. The author then refutes the claim that judgment is an act, arguing that it is a perception. The justification is introspective: after we perceive the propositional relation (whether categorical, conditional, or disjunctive), we only find ourselves perceiving that the relation is actual (corresponding to reality) or not actual. This perception is not an act but a passive reception (infi'al). The terms like 'predication' and 'affirming' are merely linguistic expressions; the underlying reality is perception. The passage further clarifies that if perception is defined as the soul being imprinted with a form, it is clearly an impression. If defined as the form itself in the soul, it belongs to the category of quality, which is not an act either. Thus either way, judgment cannot be an act.

الفعل

Key Takeaway
Judgment is a perception of truth correspondence, not an act; this is confirmed by introspection and conceptual analysis.

Critique of the Sevenfold Partition of Tasdiq
Author critiques a partition that leads to seven tasdiqat from a single proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in dividing tasdiq into multiple independent acts and explain why the proposed division leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Tasdiq cannot be subdivided; splitting it into seven acts confuses definition with proof.

In this passage, the author critiques a specific attempt to decompose the act of tasdiq (assent) into multiple components. Proponents of this division claim that a proposition like 'Man is a writer' contains several distinct acts of assent. The author shows that this leads to a multiplication of tasdiqat and fundamentally misrepresents the relationship between tasdiq, judgment, and proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being critiqued
What is the partition that allegedly leads to seven tasdiqat from a single proposition? The passage mentions the division of a proposition into subject, predicate, and relation, each giving rise to a separate tasdiq.
2
Trace the consequences of the division
Under this division, the sum of conceptions accompanying the judgment becomes a fourth tasdiq, and every pair of conceptions another, resulting in seven total. Follow how each combination supposedly yields a new assent.
3
Locate the author's objection
The author argues that this division does not align with either the philosophers' view or Imam al-Razi's view. Moreover, tasdiq would then be derived from an explanatory statement (qawl sharih), while judgment is derived from proof (hujja), which is false. This reveals a confusion between the roles of definition and demonstration.
4
Evaluate the critique
Determine why the critique is valid: it exposes that the division leads to an absurd multiplication and contradicts the proper understanding of tasdiq as a single, unitary act that comes from proof, not from conceptual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكون مجموع هذه التصورات المقارنة للحكم تصديقاً رابعاً، ويكون كل اثنين من هذه التصورات تصديقاً آخر ; فيرتقي عدد التصديقات في مثل قولك : الإنسان كاتب على مقتضى تقسيمه إلى سبعة، ويكون الحكم في كل واحد منها خارجاً عن التصديق مجامعاً له، فلايكون تقسيمه منطبقاً على شيء من المذهبين، بل لايكون صحيحاً في نفسه، لأن التصديق على هذا التفسير يكون مستفاداً من القول الشارح ويكون ما يجامعه ويقترن به - أعني الحكم - مستفاداً من الحجة، وهذا باطل.
And the sum of these conceptions accompanying the judgment becomes a fourth tasdiq, and every two of these conceptions become another tasdiq; thus the number of tasdiqat in your statement 'Man is a writer' rises to seven according to its division, and the judgment in each of them is external to the tasdiq yet concomitant with it. So his division does not correspond to either of the two schools, nor is it correct in itself, because tasdiq on this interpretation is derived from the explanatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih) and what accompanies it—namely, the judgment—is derived from proof (al-hujja). And this is false.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a particular division of tasdiq into multiple components. The author argues that if we take a simple proposition like 'Man is a writer' and try to break it down into separate tasdiqat for each component (subject, predicate, relation), we end up with seven distinct tasdiqat. However, this is problematic because it entails that tasdiq is derived from a definition (qawl sharih) while the judgment comes from proof. This contradicts the proper understanding of tasdiq, which should be a single ascertainment. The author shows that this division does not align with either the view of the philosophers (al-hukama') or that of Imam al-Razi.
