The Ambiguity of Tasawwur: Resolving the Objection on Conception and Assent
Author resolves an objection about the role of 'adam al-hukm in tasawwur within tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of tasawwur and explain how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction of including the absence of judgment (ʿadam al-ḥukm) in tasdiq.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is ambiguous; absolute tasawwur includes tasdiq; the attribute of lacking judgment does not enter tasdiq.

This passage tackles a central puzzle in Islamic logic: if tasawwur is defined by the absence of judgment, how can it be part of tasdiq, which is defined by judgment? The commentator navigates this by clarifying the polysemy of tasawwur and then refining the answer with a crucial distinction between an entity and its attributes. Understanding this resolution is key to correctly applying the division of knowledge in later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
The objector argues that if tasawwur is defined as having no judgment, then including it in tasdiq (which has judgment) would force tasdiq to contain both judgment and its absence, an impossibility. Locate this argument in the first lines of the source.
Focus on the two horns of the dilemma regarding the definition of 'judgment'.
2
Understand the first answer: two senses of tasawwur
The commentator responds that 'tasawwur' is ambiguous. It can mean simple conception (conditioned by absence of judgment) or absolute mental presence (unconditioned). Tasdiq uses the latter, not the former. See the threefold division: conditioned by judgment, conditioned by nothing, unconditioned.
The key is that tasdiq is a part of absolute tasawwur, not its opposite.
3
Recognize the residual problem
The objector persists: each component of tasdiq (subject, predicate, relation) is itself a simple conception, which is defined by lacking judgment. So tasdiq must include that absence. Identify this objection in the 'fīhi baḥth' section.
This is a deeper problem because it seems to reintroduce 'adam al-ḥukm at the level of the parts.
4
Grasp the refined answer: essence vs. attribute
The final answer distinguishes between the essence of a simple conception and its attribute. The absence of judgment is an attribute of the conception, but only the essence (the mental image itself) enters tasdiq, not the attribute. Analogies: pieces of wood (essence) vs. being cut (attribute); purification (essence) vs. not being prayer (attribute).
This saves tasdiq from containing opposite attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم، فإن عُني به الحضور الذهني مطلقاً : لزم انقسام الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره - لأنّ «الحضور الذهني مطلقاً» نفس العلم - وإن عني به المقيد بعدم الحكم، امتنع اعتبار التصوّر في التصديق، لأن عدم الحكم حينئذ يكون معتبراً في التصوّر ، فلو كان التصور معتبراً في التصديق لكان عدم الحكم معتبراً فيه - والحكم معتبر فيه أيضاً - فيلزم اعتبار الحكم وعدمه في التصديق، وإنه محال. وجوابه : أن التصوّر يطلق بالاشتراك على ما اعتبر فيه عدم الحكم - وهو التصوّر الساذج - وعلى الحضور الذهني مطلقاً - كما وقع التنبيه عليه - والمعتبر في التصديق ليس هو الأول، بل الثاني. والحاصل أن الحضور الذهني مطلقاً هو العلم، والتصوّر إما أن يعتبر بشرط شيء - أي الحكم - ويقال له التصديق، أو بشرط لاشيء - أي عدم الحكم - ويقال له التصوّر الساذج، أو لا بشرط شيء وهو مطلق التصور ; فالمقابل للتصديق هو التصوّر بشرط لاشيء، والمعتبر للتصديق عنده كما صرح به هو التصور فقط، وليس التصديق قسماً منه، بل هو قسم من التصوّر مطلقاً، فاندفع الاعتراض الأول، فلا يلزم أن يكون قسم الشيء قسيماً له. وكذا المعتبر في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - هو التصور مطلقاً – لا التصوّر فقط - وعدم الحكم إنما اعتبر في التصوّر فقط، لا في التصور مطلقاً؛ فاندفع الاعتراض الثاني أيضاً. فيه بحث، لأن المعتبر في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - هو تصوّر المحكوم عليه و تصور المحكوم به وتصوّر النسبة الحكميّة، وكل واحد من هذه التصورات تصور خاص مستفاد من القول الشارح إذا كان نظرياً، فيكون كل واحد منها تصوراً ساذجاً مقابلاً للتصديق ومندرجاً تحت مطلق التصور، فقد اعتبر في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - التصور الذي اعتبر فيه عدم الحكم، فالإشكال باق بحاله. والجواب أن يقال : إن عدم الحكم معتبر في التصور الساذج على أنه صفة له وقيد فيه، والمعتبر في التصديق هو ذات التصوّر الساذج لا صفته وقيده، فإن الموصوف إذا كان جزء من الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون صفته جزء منه، ألا ترى أن قطع الخشب أجزاء للسرير وليس كون تلك القطع جزء منه؛ وكذا الحال في الشرط، فإنّ الموصوف إذا كان شرطاً للشيء لا يجب أن يكون صفته شرطاً له؛ فإذا قلت : «الإنسان كاتب» فجزء هذا التصديق أو شرطه هو تصور الإنسان، وهذا التصوّر في نفسه موصوف بعدم الحكم، لأن الحكم لم يعرض له ، بل إنما عرض للمجموع الإدراكات الثلاث، لكن هذه الصفة خارجة عن ماهية التصديق، وموصوفها - وهو ذات ذلك التصور - داخل فيه، فلا يلزم تركيب التصديق من الحكم ونقيضه، بل من الحكم والموصوف بنقيضه؛ ولا استحالة في ذلك - فإنّ كل واحد من أجزاء البيت موصوف بنقيض الآخر - وكذا موصوفها شرط لتحقق الحكم دون الصفة، فلا يلزم اشتراط الشيء بنقيضه - بل بالموصوف بنقيضه - ولا استحالة في ذلك أيضاً، فإن شرط الصلاة - كالطهارة مثلاً - موصوف بأنه ليس بصلاة. هذا هو التحقيق الذي أفاده الشارح - قدس سرّه - في شرحه للمطالع وإنما بنى الكلام هاهنا على ما هو ظاهر الحال في التقسيمات، من أن المعتبر في كل قسم هو مورد القسمة تقريباً إلى فهم المبتدي.
As for judgment (hukm), if what is meant by it is absolute mental presence, then that necessitates the division of a thing into itself and other than itself—because 'absolute mental presence' is knowledge itself. And if what is meant by it is that which is restricted by the absence of judgment, then it is impossible to consider tasawwur in tasdiq, because the absence of judgment would then be considered in tasawwur. So if tasawwur were considered in tasdiq, then the absence of judgment would be considered in it—while judgment is also considered in it—so it would necessitate considering judgment and its absence in tasdiq, and that is impossible. The answer to it is: tasawwur is used homonymously for that in which the absence of judgment is considered—that is simple conception (al-tasawwur al-sādhij)—and for absolute mental presence—as has been pointed out—and what is considered in tasdiq is not the first, but the second. The upshot is that absolute mental presence is knowledge, and tasawwur is either considered as conditioned by something—i.e., judgment—and it is called tasdiq, or conditioned by nothing—i.e., absence of judgment—and it is called simple conception, or unconditioned (lā bi-sharṭ) and that is absolute tasawwur; so what is opposed to tasdiq is 'tasawwur conditioned by nothing', and what is considered for tasdiq according to him, as he stated, is tasawwur only, and tasdiq is not a part of it, rather it is a part of absolute tasawwur. Thus the first objection is refuted, and it does not follow that a part of a thing is opposed to it. Likewise, what is considered in tasdiq—as a condition or as a part—is absolute tasawwur, not tasawwur only, and the absence of judgment is only considered in tasawwur only, not in absolute tasawwur; so the second objection is also refuted. There is discussion [about this], because what is considered in tasdiq—as a condition or as a part—is the conception of the subject, the conception of the predicate, and the conception of the relational judgment, and each of these conceptions is a specific conception derived from the explanatory statement if it is theoretical, so each of them is a simple conception opposed to tasdiq and subsumed under absolute tasawwur. Thus what is considered in tasdiq—as a condition or as a part—is the conception in which the absence of judgment is considered, so the problem remains. The answer is to say: the absence of judgment is considered in simple conception as an attribute and a restriction thereof, but what is considered in tasdiq is the essence of the simple conception, not its attribute and restriction. For when the described thing is a part of something, it does not necessitate that its attribute be a part of it. Do you not see that pieces of wood are parts of a bed, but the fact that those pieces are cut is not a part of it? The same applies to the condition: when the described thing is a condition for something, its attribute does not have to be a condition for it. So when you say, 'Man is a writer,' the part or condition of this tasdiq is the conception of man, and this conception in itself is described by the absence of judgment, because judgment has not accrued to it, but rather accrued to the aggregate of the three perceptions. However, this attribute is external to the essence of tasdiq, while its described thing—which is the essence of that conception—is internal to it. Thus it does not necessitate that tasdiq be composed of judgment and its opposite, but rather of judgment and the thing described by its opposite. And there is no impossibility in that—for every part of a house is described by the opposite of the other. Similarly, their described thing is a condition for the realization of judgment, not the attribute. So it does not necessitate conditioning a thing by its opposite—but by the thing described by its opposite—and there is no impossibility in that either. For the condition of prayer—like purification, for example—is described as not being prayer. This is the verification that the commentator—may his secret be sanctified—conveyed in his commentary on al-Mutāli‘, and he built the discussion here on what is apparent in divisions, that what is considered in each part is the subject of the division, as an approximation for the understanding of the beginner.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the commentator addresses objections concerning the relationship between tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), specifically regarding the role of 'adam al-hukm (absence of judgment). The text begins by presenting an objection: if by 'judgment' we mean absolute mental presence, then it would be identical to knowledge itself, causing a thing to be divided into itself and something else—an impossibility. If we mean judgment restricted by the absence of judgment, then since tasawwur is defined as having absence of judgment, if tasawwur were part of tasdiq, then tasdiq would contain both judgment and its absence, which is contradictory. The answer clarifies that 'tasawwur' is ambiguous: it can mean simple conception (conditioned by absence of judgment) or absolute mental presence. Tasdiq uses the latter, not the former. The commentator then explains a threefold division: tasawwur can be considered 'conditioned by something' (i.e., judgment, which is tasdiq), 'conditioned by nothing' (simple conception), or 'unconditioned' (absolute tasawwur). Thus tasdiq is a kind of absolute tasawwur, not its opposite. A second objection is raised: in tasdiq, the conceptions of subject, predicate, and judgment relation are each simple conceptions (with absence of judgment), so how can absence of judgment be absent from tasdiq? The refined answer distinguishes between the essence of a simple conception and its attribute. The absence of judgment is an attribute of the conception, but only the essence of that conception enters into tasdiq, not its attribute. This is illustrated with examples: pieces of wood are parts of a bed, but their being cut is not; the condition of prayer (purification) is not itself prayer. Thus no contradiction arises. The commentator concludes that this is the verification from Sharh al-Mutāli‘, built on the principle that in divisions, what is considered in each part is the subject of the division, approximated for beginners.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
حكم
حضور ذهني
تصور ساذج

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Resolving the Objection on Tasawwur in Tasdiq
Author resolves an objection on tasawwur in tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the objection that tasdiq requires a tasawwur qualified by the absence of judgment by distinguishing between the essence of a concept and its attribute.

Core Idea
A concept's attribute of lacking judgment is not part of its essence; tasdiq uses the essence, not the attribute.

In defining tasdiq (assent), a subtle objection arises: if tasdiq includes bare concepts (tasawwurat) that themselves lack judgment, does that not force tasdiq to be composed of judgment and its opposite? The author of this passage provides a precise logical distinction—between the essence of a concept and its attribute—to dissolve the objection. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing the structure of knowledge statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
First, locate the objection: tasdiq requires three concepts (subject, predicate, relational judgment), each of which is a bare concept devoid of judgment. If tasdiq is defined as a concept 'without any condition' (la bi-shart shay'), then the presence of these bare concepts would introduce the very absence of judgment into tasdiq, creating a contradiction.
The objection hinges on the idea that the absence of judgment is an attribute of these concepts.
2
Understand the Author's Distinction
The author distinguishes between the essence (dhat) of a bare concept and its attribute (sifa) of lacking judgment. In tasdiq, only the essence of the concept enters, not its attribute. He uses the analogy of pieces of wood being parts of a bed: the pieces are parts, but their attribute of 'being pieces' is not part of the bed.
The same applies to conditions: the qualified thing (mawsuf) can be a condition without its attribute being a condition.
3
Apply the Distinction to the Assent Statement
Take the example 'al-insanu katibun' (The human is a writer). The concept of 'human' is a part of this assent. That concept in itself is qualified by the absence of judgment, but that qualification does not enter the essence of the assent. Thus, tasdiq is composed of judgment (the affirmation) and the qualified thing (the concept), not of judgment and its opposite.
This avoids any impossibility of composing opposites.
4
Generalize the Principle
The principle extends to conditions: the condition for prayer (tahara) is itself not prayer, i.e., qualified by its opposite. Yet it is a valid condition. So a thing can be conditioned by something qualified by its opposite without absurdity.
The author notes that this is a pedagogical simplification for beginners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - هو «التصوّر لا بشرط شيء» فلا إشكال. (1) فيه بحث، لأن المعتبر في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - هو تصوّر المحكوم عليه و تصور المحكوم به وتصوّر النسبة الحكمية، وكل واحد من هذه التصورات تصور خاص مستفاد من القول الشارح إذا كان نظرياً، فيكون كل واحد منها تصوراً ساذجاً مقابلاً للتصديق ومندرجاً تحت مطلق التصور، فقد اعتبر في التصديق - شرطاً أو شطراً - التصور الذي اعتبر فيه عدم الحكم، فالإشكال باق بحاله. والجواب أن يقال : إن عدم الحكم معتبر في التصور الساذج على أنه صفة له وقيد فيه، والمعتبر في التصديق هو ذات التصوّر الساذج لاصفته وقيده، فإن الموصوف إذا كان جزء من الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون صفته جزء منه، الاترى أن قطع الخشب أجزاء للسرير وليس كون تلك القطع جزء منه؛ وكذا الحال في الشرط، فإنّ الموصوف إذا كان شرطاً للشيء لا يجب أن تكون صفته شرطاً له؛ فإذا قلت : «الإنسان كاتب فجزء هذا التصديق أو شرطه هو تصور الإنسان، وهذا التصوّر في نفسه موصوف بعدم الحكم، لأن الحكم لم يعرض له ، بل إنما عرض المجموع الإدراكات الثلاث، لكن هذه الصفة خارجة عن ماهية التصديق، وموصوفها - وهو ذات ذلك التصور - داخل فيه، فلايلزم تركيب التصديق من الحكم ونقيضه، بل من الحكم والموصوف بنقيضه؛ ولا استحالة في ذلك - فإنّ كل واحد من أجزاء البيت موصوف بنقيض الآخر - وكذا موصوفها شرط لتحقق الحكم دون الصفة، فلا يلزم اشتراط الشيء بنقيضه - بل بالموصوف بنقيضه - ولا استحالة في ذلك أيضاً، فإن شرط الصلاة - كالطهارة مثلاً - موصوف بأنه ليس بصلاة. هذا هو التحقيق الذي أفاده الشارح - قدس سرّه - في شرحه للمطالع وإنما بنى الكلام هاهنا على ما هو ظاهر الحال في التقسيمات، من أن المعتبر في كل قسم هو مورد القسمة تقريباً إلى فهم المبتدي، فمن شنع عليه في أمثال هذه المواضع فذلك من جهله بعلو حاله أو طمعه من الجهلة اعتقاد رفعة شأنه بتزييف مقاله (شریف).
In assent (tasdiq) — whether as a condition or as a part — it is 'the concept (tasawwur) without any condition', so there is no problem. [1] There is a discussion, because what is considered in assent — whether as a condition or as a part — is the concept of the subject of the judgment, the concept of the predicate, and the concept of the relational judgment, and each of these concepts is a particular concept derived from the explanatory statement if it is theoretical, so each of them is a bare concept opposite to assent and falling under absolute concept. Thus, in assent — whether as a condition or as a part — the concept in which the absence of judgment is considered has been taken into account, so the problem remains as it is. The answer is to say: The absence of judgment is considered in the bare concept as an attribute and a restriction of it, but what is considered in assent is the essence of the bare concept, not its attribute and restriction. For if the qualified thing is a part of something, it does not necessarily follow that its attribute is a part of it. Do you not see that pieces of wood are parts of a bed, but the fact that those pieces are such is not a part of it? Likewise for the condition: if the qualified thing is a condition for something, its attribute does not have to be a condition for it. So if you say: 'The human is a writer,' the part of this assent or its condition is the concept of human, and this concept in itself is qualified by the absence of judgment, because judgment has not occurred to it; rather, it occurred to the aggregate of the three perceptions. But this attribute is external to the essence of assent, while its qualified thing — which is the essence of that concept — is internal to it. Therefore, it does not necessitate the composition of assent from judgment and its opposite, but rather from judgment and the qualified thing with its opposite. And there is no impossibility in that — for every one of the parts of a house is qualified by the opposite of the other. Likewise, the qualified thing of [the attribute] is a condition for the realization of the judgment, not the attribute. So it does not necessitate conditioning a thing by its opposite — but rather by the qualified thing with its opposite — and there is no impossibility in that either. For the condition of prayer — such as purity, for example — is qualified by not being prayer. This is the verification that the commentator — may his secret be sanctified — stated in his commentary on al-Matali‘. He only built the discussion here on what is apparent in the classifications, that what is considered in each division is the object of division, approximating to the understanding of the beginner. So whoever criticizes him in such matters, that is due to his ignorance of his high station or his greed from the ignorant thinking that his status is elevated by falsifying his statement (Sharif).
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection-and-answer in the context of defining tasdiq (assent) and its relationship to tasawwur (concept). The author begins by restating the definition of tasdiq as 'a concept without any condition' and claims there is no problem. However, an objector raises a discussion: In tasdiq, what is considered is the concept of the subject, predicate, and the relational judgment. Each of these is a bare concept that falls under absolute concept and is opposite to tasdiq. Since these concepts themselves are devoid of judgment (i.e., they are not yet assented to), the objection is that tasdiq would then include the very lack of judgment, leading to a contradiction. The author answers by distinguishing between the essence of a bare concept and its attribute (the absence of judgment). In tasdiq, only the essence of the concept enters, not its attribute. He illustrates with examples: Pieces of wood are parts of a bed, but their attribute (being such pieces) is not part of the bed. Similarly, the concept of 'human' is a part of the judgment 'Human is a writer', but the attribute of that concept (absence of judgment) is not part of the tasdiq. He further explains that the qualified thing (the concept) can be a condition for judgment without its attribute being a condition, using the example of purity (tahara) as a condition for prayer: purity is itself not prayer, so it is a condition qualified by its opposite. Thus, no impossibility arises. The author concludes by noting that the commentator based this exposition on a pedagogical approach, simplifying for beginners, and dismisses critics as ignorant.

Classifying Knowledge: Badihi and Nazari
Defining badihi and nazari with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given piece of knowledge as badihi or nazari by identifying its dependence on reflection.

Core Idea
Badihi knowledge is immediate; nazari knowledge requires effort.

In Islamic logic, knowledge is divided into two fundamental categories: self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). This distinction applies to both simple conceptions (tasawwur) and judgments (tasdiq). The author defines each type with concrete examples, giving you a tool to analyze any knowledge claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the knowledge type
Determine whether the item is a conception (simple mental representation) or an assent (judgment of truth/falsity).
Conceptions are like 'heat' or 'soul'; assents are like 'the world is created'.
2
Check for dependence on reflection
Ask: Does this knowledge require prior thought, inference, or acquisition? If yes, it is nazari; if no, it is badihi.
The passage states badihi does not depend on reflection, nazari does.
3
Apply the test to examples
Use the author's own examples: conception of heat (badihi), conception of soul (nazari), assent to non-contradiction (badihi), assent that world is created (nazari).
Verify that the classification matches the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : وليس الكلّ من كلّ منهما بديهياً وإلا لما جهلنا شيئاً، ولا نظرياً وإلا لدار أو تسلسل. أقول : العلم إما بديهي وهو الذي لم يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور الحرارة والبرودة وكالتصديق بأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان. وإما نظري، وهو الذي يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور العقل والنفس، وكالتصديق بأن العالم حادث. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : ليس كلّ واحد من كل واحد من التصوّر والتصديق بديهياً، فإنه لو كان جميع التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما كان شيء من الأشياء مجهولاً لنا - وهو باطل.
He said: Not every one of each of them is self-evident, otherwise we would not be ignorant of anything; nor is it all theoretical, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. I say: Knowledge is either self-evident, which is that whose attainment does not depend on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of heat and cold, and like the assent that negation and affirmation do not coexist and are not both removed; or theoretical, which is that whose attainment depends on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of intellect and soul, and like the assent that the world is temporally originated. If you know this, then we say: Not every single one of conception and assent is self-evident, for if all conceptions and assents were self-evident, nothing would be unknown to us—and that is false.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental divisions of knowledge: self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari), applied to both conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author first states the general claim that not all knowledge falls into either category exclusively; if all were self-evident, we would know everything, which is false, and if all were theoretical, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress in acquiring knowledge. He then defines badihi as that which requires no prior reflection or intellectual effort, giving examples of simple conceptions like heat and cold, and basic assents like the law of non-contradiction. Nazari is defined as dependent on reflection, with examples like the conception of the soul or the assent that the world is created. Finally, he argues that not all conceptions or assents are self-evident, using the reductio: if they were, nothing would be unknown, contradicting obvious human ignorance.

نظري
تصور
تصديق

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is fundamentally divided into self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari), and this division applies to both conceptions and assents.

The Reductio Against Universal Self-Evidence
Author argues against universal badihi using reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's reductio argument that not all knowledge is self-evident, using conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
If all knowledge were badihi, ignorance would be impossible — but we are ignorant.

The author does not merely define knowledge types; he argues that it cannot be that all knowledge is self-evident. His argument is a conditional proof (modus tollens) that demonstrates the necessity of theoretical knowledge. Understanding this logical move sharpens your ability to recognize when an argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The claim under attack: 'All knowledge is self-evident (badihi).' The author aims to refute this.
2
Extract the conditional
The author states: 'If all knowledge were self-evident, nothing would be unknown to us.' This is a conditional (if P then Q).
P = all knowledge is badihi; Q = nothing is unknown.
3
Deny the consequent
The author then asserts that we are ignorant of many things (the consequent Q is false). This is an implicit observation about the world.
The text says 'and that is false' referring to 'nothing would be unknown'.
4
Apply modus tollens
From 'if P then Q' and 'not Q', we conclude 'not P'. Therefore, not all knowledge is self-evident.
This is a valid logical form: modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : وليس الكلّ من كلّ منهما بديهياً وإلا لما جهلنا شيئاً، ولا نظرياً وإلا لدار أو تسلسل. أقول : العلم إما بديهي وهو الذي لم يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور الحرارة والبرودة وكالتصديق بأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان. وإما نظري، وهو الذي يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب كتصور العقل والنفس، وكالتصديق بأن العالم حادث. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : ليس كلّ واحد من كل واحد من التصوّر والتصديق بديهياً، فإنه لو كان جميع التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما كان شيء من الأشياء مجهولاً لنا - وهو باطل.
He said: Not every one of each of them is self-evident, otherwise we would not be ignorant of anything; nor is it all theoretical, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. I say: Knowledge is either self-evident, which is that whose attainment does not depend on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of heat and cold, and like the assent that negation and affirmation do not coexist and are not both removed; or theoretical, which is that whose attainment depends on reflection and acquisition, like the conception of intellect and soul, and like the assent that the world is temporally originated. If you know this, then we say: Not every single one of conception and assent is self-evident, for if all conceptions and assents were self-evident, nothing would be unknown to us—and that is false.

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental divisions of knowledge: self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari), applied to both conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author first states the general claim that not all knowledge falls into either category exclusively; if all were self-evident, we would know everything, which is false, and if all were theoretical, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress in acquiring knowledge. He then defines badihi as that which requires no prior reflection or intellectual effort, giving examples of simple conceptions like heat and cold, and basic assents like the law of non-contradiction. Nazari is defined as dependent on reflection, with examples like the conception of the soul or the assent that the world is created. Finally, he argues that not all conceptions or assents are self-evident, using the reductio: if they were, nothing would be unknown, contradicting obvious human ignorance.

Refining Badihi Tasdiq: Judgment vs. Terms
Objection and answer refining badihi tasdiq definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between self-evidence of a judgment in itself and self-evidence of its components.

Core Idea
A self-evident judgment does not require all its terms to be self-evident.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: how can something be self-evident yet unknown? The key is separating the judgment's own epistemic independence from the knowledge of its terms. This refinement is essential for correctly applying the concept of badihi tasdiq in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that something could be self-evident (badihi) yet unknown to us, because we may need to reflect on its terms. This challenges any definition of badihi that requires no reflection at all.
The objector's point hinges on conflating the judgment with its parts.
2
Distinguish direct vs. indirect dependence
The response clarifies that self-evidence means the judgment itself does not depend on reflection (tawaqquf bi-dhatihi). Any need to reflect on the terms is indirect (bi-l-wasita) and does not affect the judgment's intrinsic self-evidence.
This distinction is encapsulated in the term tawaqquf bi-l-wāsiṭa.
3
Apply to the composite view of tasdiq
If tasdiq is defined as a composite whole (majmūʿ murakkab) including both judgment and its terms (as al-Razi held), then the objection becomes stronger. But if tasdiq is simply the act of assent, only the judgment's independence matters.
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's view amplifies the challenge, but the response still holds by focusing on the judgment alone.
4
Recognize the consequence for definition
The passage refines the definition of badihi tasdiq: it is self-evident in its own essence, even if its subject and predicate require reflection. This avoids the objection without restricting badihi to simple propositions.
This refinement is common in later logical works to defend the possibility of self-evident theological knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر : لجواز أن يكون الشيء بديهياً ومجهولاً لنا، فإنّ النظر كان بديهياً داخلاً في تعريفه لأنه لم يتوقف في ذاته على نظر، وهذا هو المراد تما ذكر في تعريفه ؛ وأما توقفه على النظر في أطرافه، فذلك توقف بالواسطة، وإذا جعل التصديق عبارة عن المجموع المركب - كما هو مذهب الإمام - قوى هذا الإشكال.
And there is an objection: it is possible for something to be self-evident yet unknown to us, because a self-evident thing is included in its definition since it does not depend in itself on reflection—that is what is meant in its definition; as for its dependence on reflection regarding its terms, that is dependence by intermediary. And if tasdiq is made to be the composite whole—as is the view of the Imam—this objection is strengthened.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the definition of a self-evident assent (tasdiq badihi). The objection states that something could be self-evident yet unknown, because self-evidence applies to the act of judgment itself, not necessarily to the conception of its terms. The response clarifies that self-evidence means the judgment does not depend on reflection in its own essence; any dependence on reflection regarding its subject or predicate is indirect (bi-l-wasīṭa). The objection gains force if tasdiq is defined as a composite whole (including both the judgment and its terms), as Fakhr al-Din al-Razi held. The passage thus refines the definition of badihi tasdiq by distinguishing between the judgment's intrinsic independence and the need to understand its components.

Key Terms
توقف بالواسطة
مجموع مركب

Checkpoint
According to the response in the passage, what does it mean for something to be self-evident (badihi)?
A) It requires no reflection on any of its parts.
B) It does not depend on reflection in its own essence, though it may depend on reflection for its terms.
C) It must have self-evident terms.
D) It is only self-evident if both the judgment and its terms are known.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of a judgment concerns its own independence from reflection, not the self-evidence of its constituent terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (al-‘ālam ḥādith). According to the distinction in this passage, in what sense could this be considered badihi for a theologian? Explain whether the judgment itself requires reflection or only the terms.
?
In a debate, an opponent says: 'The existence of God is not self-evident because the term "God" is not self-evident.' Using the tools from this lesson, construct a response that defends the self-evidence of the judgment while acknowledging the need for conceptual knowledge.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
7 min
Not All Tasawwur and Tasdiq Are Self-Evident: The Initial Claim and Objection
Author challenges the claim that all tasawwur and tasdiq are badihi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the initial claim that not all tasawwur and tasdiq are badihi and identify the objection that something can be badihi yet unknown.

Core Idea
Self-evidence does not guarantee actual knowledge; prerequisites may be needed.

This lesson examines the opening argument that not every concept and assent is self-evident. We will see an objection and the clarification that self-evidence is about the essence, not about actual possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The author says: Not every tasawwur and tasdiq is badihi. The justification is that if all were badihi, nothing would be unknown.
2
Examine the objection
The objection says something can be badihi yet unknown. The example of nazar (reflection) illustrates this: nazar is badihi in its definition but depends on reflection for its parts.
This shows badihi in itself vs. actual attainment.
3
Understand the dependency
The objection explains that badihi in itself may depend on something else (attention, sense, etc.) for its attainment.
Thus, self-evidence does not entail actual knowledge.
4
See the strengthening with tasdiq as composite
If tasdiq is taken as a composite whole (al-Razi's view), the objection becomes stronger because the components may not be self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : ليس كلّ واحد من كل واحد من التصوّر والتصديق بديهياً، فإنه لو كان جميع التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما كان شيء من الأشياء مجهولاً لنا - وهو باطل.
وفيه نظر : لجواز أن يكون الشيء بديهياً ومجهولاً لنا، فإنّ النظر كان بديهياً داخلاً في تعريفه لأنه لم يتوقف في ذاته على نظر، وهذا هو المراد كما ذكر في تعريفه ؛ وأما توقفه على النظر في أطرافه، فذلك توقف بالواسطة، وإذا جعل التصديق عبارة عن المجموع المركب - كما هو مذهب الإمام - قوى هذا الإشكال (شريف).
1) يريد أنه ليس كل واحد من التصورات بديهياً ولا كل واحد منها نظرياً حتى يلزم أن بعض التصورات بديهي وبعضها نظري، وكذلك ليس كل واحد من التصديقات بديهياً ولا كل واحد منها نظرياً حتى يلزم أن بعضها بديهي وبعضها نظري لكنه جمع بين التصورات والتصديقات اختصاراً في العبارة مع الاشتراك في الدليل، والمراد ما ذكرناه، فكأنه قال : ليس جميع التصورات بديهياً، وإلا لما احتجنا إلى نظر في تحصيل شيء من التصورات – وهو باطل قطعاً - وكذلك ليس جميع التصديقات بديهياً، وإلا لما احتجنا في تحصيل شيء من التصديقات إلى نظر، وهو أيضاً باطل قطعاً (شريف).
(۲) هذا النظر وارد على ظاهر هذه العبارة وإن كان المصنف قد فسرها في شرح الكشف بعدم الاحتياج إلى النظر. قال بعض الأفاضل في توجيه هذا التفسير : يعني لما كان شيء من الأشياء مجهولاً لنا جهلاً محوجاً إلى نظر، فكان ما لا يحتاج إلى نظر معلوماً لنا» - فتأمل (شريف).
البديهي وإن لم يتوقف حصوله على نظر وكسب، لكن يمكن أن يتوقف حصوله على شيء آخر - من توجه العقل إليه أو الإحساس به أو الحدس أو التجربة أو غير ذلك - فما لم يحصل ذلك الشيء الموقوف عليه لم يحصل البديهي، فالبداهة لا تستلزم الحصول.
فالصواب أن يقال : لو كان كلّ واحد من التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما احتجنا في تحصيل شيء من الأشياء إلى كسب ونظر، وهو فاسد - ضرورة احتياجنا في تحصيل بعض التصورات والتصديقات إلى الفكر والنظر - ولا نظرياً أي ليس كل واحد من التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصورات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل؛ وكذلك ليس كل واحد من التصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصديقات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل، وإنما جمع بينهما للاشتراك في الدليل والاختصار، على قياس ما مر. فإن قلت : جاز أن يكون جميع التصورات نظرياً وتنتهي سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصديق بديهي، فلا يلزم الدور ولا التسلسل، وجاز أيضاً أن يكون جميع التصديقات نظرياً، وتنتهي سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصور بديهي، فلا دور ولا تسلسل أيضاً.
قلت : هذا البرهان موقوف على امتناع اكتساب التصورات من التصديقات وبالعكس، فإن تم، تم الكلام وإلا فلا؛ على أن البيان في التصورات يتم بدون ذلك أيضاً، لأن التصديق البديهي الذي ينتهي إليه اكتساب التصورات موقوف على تصوّر المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والنسبة الحكمية، وكلّ ذلك
If you know this, we say: Not every single one of tasawwur and tasdiq is self-evident (badihi), for if all tasawwurat and tasdiqat were self-evident, nothing would be unknown to us — and that is false. And there is an objection: because it is possible that a thing is self-evident yet unknown to us. For reflection (nazar) was self-evident in entering its definition because it did not depend in itself on reflection, and that is what is meant as mentioned in its definition. As for its dependence on reflection in its parts, that is dependence by intermediary. And if tasdiq is made an expression for the composite whole — as is the doctrine of the Imam — this objection strengthens (Sharif). (1) He means that not every one of the tasawwurat is self-evident nor every one of them theoretical (nazari) such that it follows that some tasawwurat are self-evident and some theoretical, and likewise not every one of the tasdiqat is self-evident nor every one of them theoretical such that it follows that some are self-evident and some theoretical. But he combined tasawwurat and tasdiqat for brevity in expression with sharing the proof, and the intended meaning is what we mentioned. So it is as if he said: Not all tasawwurat are self-evident, otherwise we would not need reflection for acquiring any of the tasawwurat — and that is absolutely false. And likewise not all tasdiqat are self-evident, otherwise we would not need reflection for acquiring any of the tasdiqat — and that also is absolutely false (Sharif). (2) This objection is directed at the apparent meaning of this expression, even though the author interpreted it in Sharh al-Kashf as 'not needing reflection'. Some of the virtuosi said in directing this interpretation: He meant that since nothing of the things is unknown to us with an ignorance requiring reflection, then what does not need reflection is known to us — so reflect (Sharif). The self-evident, even if its acquisition does not depend on reflection and acquisition, can still depend on something else — from attention of the intellect to it, or sense perception, or intuition, or experience, or other — so as long as that dependent thing is not obtained, the self-evident is not obtained. Thus self-evidence does not entail actual obtaining. So the correct thing to say is: If every one of the tasawwurat and tasdiqat were self-evident, we would not need acquisition and reflection for obtaining any of the things — and that is corrupt, given our need in acquiring some tasawwurat and tasdiqat for thought and reflection. And neither are they all theoretical — meaning not every one of the tasawwurat and tasdiqat is theoretical, for if every one of them were theoretical, the acquisition of tasawwurat would be by way of circularity or infinite regress; and likewise not every one of the tasdiqat is theoretical, for if every one of them were theoretical, the acquisition of tasdiqat would be by way of circularity or infinite regress. He combined them for sharing the proof and brevity, in analogy to what came before. If you say: It is possible that all tasawwurat are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident tasdiq, so circularity and regress do not follow; and it is also possible that all tasdiqat are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident tasawwur, so there is no circularity or regress either. I say: This proof is dependent on the impossibility of acquiring tasawwurat from tasdiqat and vice versa. If that is established, the argument stands, otherwise not. Moreover, the demonstration for tasawwurat is completed without that as well, because the self-evident tasdiq at which the acquisition of tasawwurat ends is dependent on the concept of the subject, the predicate, and the judgmental relation — all of that.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a detailed epistemological refinement of the classification of knowledge into self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). It begins by stating the initial claim: not every concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) is badihi, because if all were badihi, nothing would be unknown — which is false. An objection is then raised: something can be badihi in itself yet unknown to us, because 'reflection' (nazar) is self-evident in its definition (it does not depend on reflection for its own meaning), but it may depend on reflection for grasping its parts. This objection is strengthened if tasdiq is taken as a composite whole (as per al-Imam al-Razi). The commentator clarifies that the original statement intended a different meaning: not all are badihi, and not all are nazari; otherwise, if all were badihi, we would need no effort, which is false. If all were nazari, there would be circularity or infinite regress. The objection is addressed by noting that badihi can still require prerequisites (attention, sensation, intuition, experience) for its actual attainment, so badihi does not guarantee actual knowledge. The correct formulation is: if all were badihi, we would need no acquisition, which is false because we do need it. And not all are nazari, because that would lead to circularity or regress. A further objection is considered: perhaps all tasawwur are nazari but end at a badihi tasdiq, avoiding regress. The reply is that this depends on whether tasawwur can be acquired from tasdiq and vice versa. Even if not, the demonstration for tasawwur stands because the self-evident tasdiq at the end depends on concepts of subject, predicate, and relation.

تصديق
بديهي
نظري

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the initial claim that if all tasawwur and tasdiq were badihi, nothing would be unknown?
A) The objection says that tasawwur and tasdiq are not the only types of knowledge.
B) The objection says that something can be badihi in itself yet unknown to us due to dependency on prerequisites.
C) The objection says that tasdiq is a composite whole, so it cannot be badihi.
D) The objection says that nazar is not badihi because it depends on reflection.

Key Takeaway
The initial claim that not all tasawwur and tasdiq are badihi is correct, but the first justification (if all were badihi nothing would be unknown) is challenged because something can be badihi yet unknown.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
8 min
The Refined Formulation: Why Not All Are Nazari and the Circularity Argument
Author presents the correct formulation and argues against all knowledge being theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the refined formulation that not all tasawwur and tasdiq are nazari and why all-nazari leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If all knowledge were theoretical, no foundation would exist, leading to circularity or infinite regress.

After refining the initial objection, the author presents the correct statement: not all concepts and assents are self-evident, and not all are theoretical. This lesson focuses on the argument against all being theoretical, which would entail a vicious circle or infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
State the refined formulation
The author says: the correct statement is that not all tasawwur and tasdiq are self-evident (because we need acquisition for some), and not all are theoretical (because that would lead to circularity/regress).
2
Understand the argument against all-nazari for tasawwur
If all concepts (tasawwurat) were theoretical, then acquiring any concept would require prior concepts, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
The same applies to tasdiqat.
3
Consider the counter-objection
One might say: it is possible that all tasawwurat are theoretical but the chain ends at a self-evident tasdiq, avoiding regress. Or all tasdiqat end at a self-evident tasawwur.
4
Evaluate the reply
The author replies that this depends on whether tasawwur can be acquired from tasdiq (and vice versa). Even if it could, the demonstration for tasawwur still holds because the self-evident tasdiq itself depends on concepts (subject, predicate, relation).
Thus, some tasawwur must be self-evident.

A further objection is considered: perhaps all tasawwur are nazari but end at a badihi tasdiq, avoiding regress. The reply is that this depends on whether tasawwur can be acquired from tasdiq and vice versa. Even if not, the demonstration for tasawwur stands because the self-evident tasdiq at the end depends on concepts of subject, predicate, and relation.

A) Because concepts are always self-evident.
B) Because if all concepts were theoretical, acquiring them would require prior concepts, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
C) Because theoretical knowledge depends on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The correct formulation distinguishes between badihi and nazari for both tasawwur and tasdiq, and the argument against all-nazari is that it would entail circularity or infinite regress.

The Core Reductio Against All Knowledge Being Theoretical
Author proves the necessity of self-evident knowledge via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that not all concepts and assents can be theoretical by identifying the implication of circularity or infinite regress and concluding that some knowledge must be self-evident.

Core Idea
If all knowledge required proof, nothing would ever be known.

You are about to encounter a foundational argument in Islamic epistemology. The author shows that the claim 'all concepts and assents are theoretical' logically leads to either circularity or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible. Therefore, some knowledge must be self-evident (badihi). This argument is crucial for establishing a secure foundation for all knowledge, including in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Locate the statement that 'every single concept and assent is theoretical' (نظري). This is the antecedent that the author intends to disprove.
2
Understand the hypothetical consequence
If the claim were true, then to acquire any concept or assent, we would need another theoretical knowledge, and that another, leading to an unending chain or a circle. The author describes this as 'by way of circularity or infinite regress'.
Think of a chain of dependencies: each piece of knowledge depends on a prior one.
3
Define dawr and tasalsul as given
The passage defines circularity as 'the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that thing' either directly or through steps, and infinite regress as 'the arrangement of infinite things'.
These definitions are standard in Islamic logic.
4
Apply the reductio reasoning
The author states 'The consequent is false, so the antecedent is similarly false.' Since infinite regress and circularity are impossible (the consequent is false), the claim that all are theoretical must be false.
This is a modus tollens argument.
5
State the conclusion
Therefore, not all concepts and assents are theoretical; some are self-evident (badihi). This conclusion is necessary to avoid the absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالصواب أن يقال : لوكان كلّ واحد من التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما احتجنا في تحصيل شيء من الأشياء إلى كسب ونظر، وهو فاسد - ضرورة احتياجنافي تحصيل بعض التصورات والتصديقات إلى الفكر والنظر - ولا نظرياً ) أي ليس كل واحد من التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصورات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل ؛ وكذلك ليس كل واحد من التصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصديقات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل، وإنما جمع بينهما للاشتراك في الدليل والاختصار، على قياس مامر. فإن قلت : جاز أن يكون جميع التصورات نظرياً وتنتهي سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصديق بديهي، فلايلزم الدور ولا التسلسل، وجاز أيضاً أن يكون جميع التصديقات نظرياً، وتنتهى سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصور بديهي، فلادور ولا تسلسل أيضاً. قلت : هذا البرهان موقوف على امتناع اكتساب التصورات من التصديقات وبالعكس، فإن تم، تم الكلام وإلا فلا؛ على أن البيان في التصورات يتم بدون ذلك أيضاً، لأن التصديق البديهي الذي ينتهي إليه اكتساب التصورات موقوف على تصوّر المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والنسبة الحكمية، وكلّ ذلك نظرياً ، فإنه لو كان جميع التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً لزم الدور أو التسلسل. والدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف على ذلك الشيء من جهة واحدة، إما بمرتبة - كما يتوقف «أ» على حصول «ب» وبالعكس - أو بمراتب - كما يتوقف «أ» على «ب» و «ب» على «ج» و «ج» على «أ». والتسلسل هو ترتيب أمور غير متناهية. واللازم باطل فالملزوم مثله. أما الملازمة : فلأنه على ذلك التقدير إذا حاولنا تحصيل شيء منهما ، فلابد أن يكون حصوله بعلم آخر، وذلك الآخر أيضاً نظري، فيكون حصوله بعلم آخر - وهلم جرا - فإما أن تذهب سلسلة الاكتساب إلى غير النهاية - وهو التسلسل - أوتعود فيلزم الدور. العلم نظري على ذلك التقدير؛ فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل. فإن قلت : على تقدير أن يكون جميع التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً يكون قولك : «لوكان كلّها نظرياً يلزم الدور أو التسلسل» تصديقاً نظرياً، ويكون كل واحد من التصوّرات المذكورات فيه (ن) : المذكورة) أيضاً نظرياً، ويكون أيضاً قولك : واللازم باطل والملزوم مثله» تصديقاً نظرياً، والتصورات المذكورة فيه أيضاً نظرية، فيحتاج في تحصيل هذه التصديقات والتصورات إلى الدور أو التسلسل المحالين فيكون الاستدلال بهذه المقدمات محالاً. قلت : هذه المقدمات وتصوراتها أمور معلومة لنا بلاشبهة في ذلك، فيتم الاستدلال بها قطعاً. نعم، يلزم أيضاً من كونها معلومة لنا أن لا يكون جميع التصوّرات والتصديقات نظرياً في الواقع، وهذا مؤيد لمطلوبنا (شريف).
So the correct thing to say is: If every single concept and assent were self-evident, we would not need, in obtaining any of the things, to acquire or reflect—which is false, because of our necessity, in obtaining some concepts and assents, for thinking and reflection. And not all are theoretical, i.e., not every single concept and assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of concepts would be by way of circularity or infinite regress; and likewise not every single assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of assents would be by way of circularity or infinite regress. And they are combined only for sharing in the proof and for brevity, analogous to what preceded. If you say: It is possible that all concepts are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident assent, so circularity and infinite regress do not follow; and it is also possible that all assents are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident concept, so there is no circularity or infinite regress either. I say: This proof depends on the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa; if that is established, the argument is complete, otherwise not. Moreover, the demonstration regarding concepts is complete even without that, because the self-evident assent at which the acquisition of concepts ends depends on the conception of the subject, the predicate, and the judgmental relation, and all that [conception] is theoretical. For if all concepts and assents were theoretical, circularity or infinite regress would follow. Circularity is the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that thing from one direction, either in one degree—as when A depends on obtaining B and vice versa—or in degrees—as when A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A. Infinite regress is the arrangement of infinite things. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is similarly false. As for the entailment: because on that supposition, if we try to obtain something of them, its acquisition must be through another knowledge, and that other is also theoretical, so its acquisition would be through another knowledge, and so on. Then either the chain of acquisition goes on infinitely—which is infinite regress—or it returns, resulting in circularity. [That] knowledge is theoretical on that supposition, so circularity or infinite regress follows. If you say: On the supposition that all concepts and assents are theoretical, your statement 'if all were theoretical, circularity or infinite regress would follow' is a theoretical assent, and each of the concepts mentioned in it are also theoretical, and your statement 'the consequent is false, so the antecedent is similar' is also a theoretical assent, and the concepts mentioned in it are also theoretical; so the acquisition of these assents and concepts requires the impossible circularity or infinite regress, making the inference from these premises impossible. I say: These premises and their concepts are things known to us without any doubt, so the inference with them is definitely valid. Yes, it also follows from their being known to us that not all concepts and assents are theoretical in reality, and this supports our thesis. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument establishing that not all concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) can be theoretical (nazari). The author, commenting on the matn, begins by correcting a misunderstanding: if all were self-evident (badihi), no acquisition (kasb) would be needed—this is false because we do need thought for some. Then he asserts the correct view: not all are theoretical, because if they were, acquisition would involve circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). An objector suggests that the chain could end in a self-evident concept or assent of the other type, breaking the regress. The author responds that this assumes the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa; but even without that, the argument for concepts works because a self-evident assent itself requires concepts of subject, predicate, and judgment, which would be theoretical if all were theoretical. He defines circularity and infinite regress, then proves the entailment: on the supposition that all are theoretical, any attempt to acquire knowledge leads to an infinite chain or a circle. A further objection is raised: the very premises used in this argument, if all are theoretical, would also be subject to circularity or regress, making the argument self-defeating. The author replies that these premises are known to us without doubt, so the inference stands. Moreover, their being known shows that in reality not all are theoretical, supporting the conclusion.

نظري
دور
تسلسل

Refuting the Objection of Cross-Type Regress Termination
Author counters an objection that cross-type self-evident knowledge could stop the regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that a chain of theoretical knowledge could end in a self-evident knowledge of the other type by analyzing the dependency of assents on concepts.

Core Idea
A self-evident assent still depends on concepts; if all concepts are theoretical, the regress continues.

An objector proposes that even if all concepts were theoretical, the chain could end in a self-evident assent, and vice versa. The author counters that a self-evident assent itself requires the conception of its subject, predicate, and judgment—all of which would be theoretical if all concepts were theoretical. This shows the objection fails to escape the regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says: It is possible that all concepts are theoretical, but the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident assent, and similarly for assents ending in a self-evident concept. Thus no circularity or regress.
2
Understand the author's premise
The author states that the proof depends on the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa. But even without that, the demonstration regarding concepts is complete.
3
Analyze the dependency of assents on concepts
A self-evident assent (e.g., 'The whole is greater than the part') requires conceiving the subject ('whole'), predicate ('greater than the part'), and the judgmental relation. These concepts, if all concepts are theoretical, would also be theoretical.
Thus the assent depends on theoretical concepts, so the chain does not truly end.
4
Apply the same reasoning
If all assents were theoretical, then a self-evident concept used to terminate the chain would itself depend on an assent (e.g., the concept of 'existence' may depend on the assent that 'I exist'), leading to the same problem.
5
Conclude that the objection fails
Therefore, the objection does not escape the reductio; the only way to avoid circularity/regress is to admit that some concepts or assents are self-evident from the start.

Source Text

So the correct thing to say is: If every single concept and assent were self-evident, we would not need, in obtaining any of the things, to acquire or reflect—which is false, because of our necessity, in obtaining some concepts and assents, for thinking and reflection. And not all are theoretical, i.e., not every single concept and assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of concepts would be by way of circularity or infinite regress; and likewise not every single assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of assents would be by way of circularity or infinite regress. The Self-Referential Objection and Its Resolution
Author resolves a self-referential objection to the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the self-referential objection to the proof and defend it by recognizing that the premises of the proof are self-evident.

Core Idea
The proof's own premises are known without doubt, so the argument is not self-defeating.

A sophisticated objection arises: if all knowledge were theoretical, then the very premises of this proof would themselves be theoretical, leading to the same circularity or regress. This would make the proof self-defeating. The author responds that these premises are known to us without any doubt (badihi), so they are not subject to the regress. This response reinforces the conclusion that some knowledge must be self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the self-referential objection
The objector says: On the assumption that all concepts and assents are theoretical, your own statements ('if all were theoretical, circularity or regress follows' and 'the consequent is false') are themselves theoretical assents, and their concepts are theoretical. So the argument would itself require circularity or regress to be known, making the inference invalid.
2
Understand the author's reply
The author asserts: 'These premises and their concepts are things known to us without any doubt.' That is, the premises are self-evident, not theoretical. Therefore, they are not vulnerable to the regress.
3
Recognize the meta-implication
The fact that these premises are known self-evidently itself shows that not all knowledge is theoretical. This supports the original thesis.
4
Evaluate the logical soundness
The proof does not assume its own conclusion; it uses premises that are independently evident. Thus the objection fails.

فالصواب أن يقال : لوكان كلّ واحد من التصورات والتصديقات بديهياً لما احتجنا في تحصيل شيء من الأشياء إلى كسب ونظر، وهو فاسد - ضرورة احتياجنافي تحصيل بعض التصورات والتصديقات إلى الفكر والنظر - ولا نظرياً ) أي ليس كل واحد من التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصورات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل ؛ وكذلك ليس كل واحد من التصديقات نظرياً، إذ لو كان كل واحد منها نظرياً لكان تحصيل التصديقات بطريق الدور أو التسلسل، وإنما جمع بينهما للاشتراك في الدليل والاختصار، على قياس مامر. فإن قلت : جاز أن يكون جميع التصورات نظرياً وتنتهي سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصديق بديهي، فلايلزم الدور ولا التسلسل، وجاز أيضاً أن يكون جميع التصديقات نظرياً، وتنتهى سلسلة الاكتساب إلى تصور بديهي، فلادور ولا تسلسل أيضاً. قلت : هذا البرهان موقوف على امتناع اكتساب التصورات من التصديقات وبالعكس، فإن تم، تم الكلام وإلا فلا؛ على أن البيان في التصورات يتم بدون ذلك أيضاً، لأن التصديق البديهي الذي ينتهي إليه اكتساب التصورات موقوف على تصوّر المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والنسبة الحكمية، وكلّ ذلك نظرياً ، فإنه لو كان جميع التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً لزم الدور أو التسلسل. والدور هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف على ذلك الشيء من جهة واحدة، إما بمرتبة - كما يتوقف «أ» على حصول «ب» وبالعكس - أو بمراتب - كما يتوقف «أ» على «ب» و «ب» على «ج» و «ج» على «أ». والتسلسل هو ترتيب أمور غير متناهية. واللازم باطل فالملزوم مثله. أما الملازمة : فلأنه على ذلك التقدير إذا حاولنا تحصيل شيء منهما ، فلابد أن يكون حصوله بعلم آخر، وذلك الآخر أيضاً نظري، فيكون حصوله بعلم آخر - وهلم جرا - فإما أن تذهب سلسلة الاكتساب إلى غير النهاية - وهو التسلسل - أوتعود فيلزم الدور. العلم نظري على ذلك التقدير؛ فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل. فإن قلت : على تقدير أن يكون جميع التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً يكون قولك : «لوكان كلّها نظرياً يلزم الدور أو التسلسل» تصديقاً نظرياً، ويكون كل واحد من التصوّرات المذكورات فيه (ن) : المذكورة) أيضاً نظرياً، ويكون أيضاً قولك : واللازم باطل والملزوم مثله» تصديقاً نظرياً، والتصورات المذكورة فيه أيضاً نظرية، فيحتاج في تحصيل هذه التصديقات والتصورات إلى الدور أو التسلسل المحالين فيكون الاستدلال بهذه المقدمات محالاً. قلت : هذه المقدمات وتصوراتها أمور معلومة لنا بلاشبهة في ذلك، فيتم الاستدلال بها قطعاً. نعم، يلزم أيضاً من كونها معلومة لنا أن لا يكون جميع التصوّرات والتصديقات نظرياً في الواقع، وهذا مؤيد لمطلوبنا (شريف).
So the correct thing to say is: If every single concept and assent were self-evident, we would not need, in obtaining any of the things, to acquire or reflect—which is false, because of our necessity, in obtaining some concepts and assents, for thinking and reflection. And not all are theoretical, i.e., not every single concept and assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of concepts would be by way of circularity or infinite regress; and likewise not every single assent is theoretical, for if every single one were theoretical, then the acquisition of assents would be by way of circularity or infinite regress. And they are combined only for sharing in the proof and for brevity, analogous to what preceded. If you say: It is possible that all concepts are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident assent, so circularity and infinite regress do not follow; and it is also possible that all assents are theoretical and the chain of acquisition ends at a self-evident concept, so there is no circularity or infinite regress either. I say: This proof depends on the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa; if that is established, the argument is complete, otherwise not. Moreover, the demonstration regarding concepts is complete even without that, because the self-evident assent at which the acquisition of concepts ends depends on the conception of the subject, the predicate, and the judgmental relation, and all that [conception] is theoretical. For if all concepts and assents were theoretical, circularity or infinite regress would follow. Circularity is the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that thing from one direction, either in one degree—as when A depends on obtaining B and vice versa—or in degrees—as when A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A. Infinite regress is the arrangement of infinite things. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is similarly false. As for the entailment: because on that supposition, if we try to obtain something of them, its acquisition must be through another knowledge, and that other is also theoretical, so its acquisition would be through another knowledge, and so on. Then either the chain of acquisition goes on infinitely—which is infinite regress—or it returns, resulting in circularity. [That] knowledge is theoretical on that supposition, so circularity or infinite regress follows. If you say: On the supposition that all concepts and assents are theoretical, your statement 'if all were theoretical, circularity or infinite regress would follow' is a theoretical assent, and each of the concepts mentioned in it are also theoretical, and your statement 'the consequent is false, so the antecedent is similar' is also a theoretical assent, and the concepts mentioned in it are also theoretical; so the acquisition of these assents and concepts requires the impossible circularity or infinite regress, making the inference from these premises impossible. I say: These premises and their concepts are things known to us without any doubt, so the inference with them is definitely valid. Yes, it also follows from their being known to us that not all concepts and assents are theoretical in reality, and this supports our thesis. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument establishing that not all concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) can be theoretical (nazari). The author, commenting on the matn, begins by correcting a misunderstanding: if all were self-evident (badihi), no acquisition (kasb) would be needed—this is false because we do need thought for some. Then he asserts the correct view: not all are theoretical, because if they were, acquisition would involve circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). An objector suggests that the chain could end in a self-evident concept or assent of the other type, breaking the regress. The author responds that this assumes the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa; but even without that, the argument for concepts works because a self-evident assent itself requires concepts of subject, predicate, and judgment, which would be theoretical if all were theoretical. He defines circularity and infinite regress, then proves the entailment: on the supposition that all are theoretical, any attempt to acquire knowledge leads to an infinite chain or a circle. A further objection is raised: the very premises used in this argument, if all are theoretical, would also be subject to circularity or regress, making the argument self-defeating. The author replies that these premises are known to us without doubt, so the inference stands. Moreover, their being known shows that in reality not all are theoretical, supporting the conclusion.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that his own premises are theoretical and thus subject to the same regress?
A) He admits the objection and modifies the argument.
B) He argues that the premises are known without doubt, i.e., self-evident.
C) He says the regress is not a problem for premises.
D) He claims the objection is based on a misunderstanding of theoretical knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The proof that not all knowledge is theoretical rests on self-evident premises that are themselves not theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a premise in a theological or legal argument that you consider self-evident (e.g., 'the existence of the self'). Explain why it must be self-evident to avoid infinite regress.
?
Critique a statement that claims 'all knowledge requires proof.' Use the self-referential objection to show that this claim would be self-defeating unless at least some knowledge is self-evident.

Conditional Refutation of Infinite Regress
Author refutes objection against infinite regress using soul's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument about infinite regress in knowledge and evaluate its validity based on the status of the soul.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible only if the soul is originated, not if it is eternal.

The objector claims that infinite regress in knowledge is impossible because you cannot have infinite premises in finite time. The author responds by pointing out that this depends on the soul being originated. If the soul is eternal, then infinite knowledge can be acquired over infinite time. This lesson dissects that conditional argument and the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find where the author says 'if you mean that it depends on their being present in infinite times, that is conceded...'. The objector asserts infinite knowledge is impossible.
The objector's claim is based on the assumption of a finite, originated soul.
2
Extract the condition
The author says impossibility would hold if the soul were originated (haditha), but if it is eternal (qadima), infinite knowledge in infinite time is possible.
The condition hinges on the nature of the soul.
3
Formalize the conditional
If the soul is eternal, then it is possible to have infinite sciences in infinite times. The author asserts the antecedent (soul eternal) and thus the consequent is possible.
This is a conditional with antecedent: soul eternal, consequent: infinite knowledge possible.
4
Evaluate the dialectical move
The author shifts the burden: the objector's argument relies on an unproven premise (soul originated). The author claims the soul's eternity is proven elsewhere (in hikma).
The refutation is based on denying the hidden premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عنيتم به أنه يتوقف على استحضارها في أزمنة غير متناهية فمسلّم، ولكن لانسلّم أن استحضار الأمور الغير المتناهية في الأزمنة الغير المتناهية محال ؛ وإنما يستحيل ذلك لو كانت النفس حادثة، فأما إذا كانت قديمة تكون موجودة في أزمنة غير متناهية، فجاز أن يحصل لها علوم غير متناهية في أزمنة غير متناهية. فنقول : هذا الدليل مبني على حدوث النفس، وقد برهن عليه في فن الحكمة .) - أي بالفعل - لكنّها يجب أن تجامعه مجملة - إي بالقوة القريبة – كما ذكرت في المسائل الهندسية. قلت : إدراك النفس دفعة لأمور غير متناهية مجملة غير محال، وإنما المحال إدراكها إياها دفعة مفصلة، فيجوز أن يحصل للنفس أمور غير متناهية مفصلة في أزمنة غير متناهية، وتكون تلك الأمور حاصلة لها الآن – أي عند حصول المطلوب المتوقف عليها - مجملة؛ على أنا نقول : كما جاز أن لا تكون تلك الأمور حاصلة بالفعل عند حصول المطلوب جاز أيضاً أن لا تكون حاصلة بالقوة القريبة، فلابد لنفي هذا الجواز من دليل (شريف). (1) قد يتوهم عدم ابتنائه عليه، لأن الناظر لتحصيل المطلوب إذا توجه إليه فلابد أن يحصل عنده بعد ما قصد إليه وقبل أن يحصل له – جميع ما يتوقف عليه من العلوم والإدراكات، وذلك زمان متناه فيمتنع أن يحصل فيه أمور غير متناهيه ؛ وفساده ظاهر، لأن حصول المطلوب بطريق التسلسل يستلزم أن تكون تلك الأمور حاصلة له في نفسه، ولو متعاقبة في أزمنة غير متناهية ؛ وأما إذا توجه إلى تحصيل المطلوب بالنظر فلا يجب عليه إلا ملاحظة ما هو مباد قريبة له ليتمكن من النظر ؛ وأما ملاحظة المبادئ البعيدة فلا. نعم، يجب لأن يكون قد حصل له قبل ذلك تلك المبادئ البعيدة والأنظار الواقعة فيها، ليتصور حصول المبادئ القريبة له - هذا. والأولى أن يقال : ليس جميع التصورات والتصديقات نظرياً، لأن بعض التصورات - كتصور الحرارة والبرودة وأمثالهما - وبعض التصديقات - كالتصديق بأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان وبأن الكل أعظم من الجزء ونظائرهما - حاصلة بلانظر واكتساب (شريف).
If you mean that it depends on their being present in infinite times, that is conceded, but we do not concede that the presence of infinite matters in infinite times is impossible; rather that would be impossible if the soul were originated, but if it is eternal and exists in infinite times, then it is possible for it to acquire infinite sciences in infinite times. So we say: This argument is based on the origination of the soul, and it has been demonstrated in the science of wisdom. – i.e., actually – but they must be with it in summary – i.e., in proximate potentiality – as mentioned in geometrical problems. I say: The soul's apprehension of infinite matters at once in summary is not impossible; only their apprehension at once in detail is impossible. So it is possible for the soul to have infinite detailed matters in infinite times, and those matters are present to it now – i.e., at the moment of obtaining the sought dependent on them – in summary. Moreover, we say: Just as it is possible that those matters are not actually present at the obtaining of the sought, it is also possible that they are not present in proximate potentiality; so to negate this possibility a proof is needed. (1) It might be thought that it is not based on that, because the inquirer aiming to obtain the sought, when he directs himself to it, must have present to him after intending it and before obtaining it all the sciences and perceptions on which it depends, and that is a finite time, so it is impossible that infinite matters occur in it. But its invalidity is clear, because obtaining the sought by way of regression entails that those matters are present in himself, even if successively in infinite times. And when he directs himself to obtain the sought by reflection, he is only required to observe what are its proximate principles to be able to reflect; as for observing distant principles, no. Indeed, it is necessary that before that he had obtained those distant principles and the reflections occurring in them, so that he can conceive obtaining the proximate principles – that is it. And it is better to say: Not all concepts and assents are theoretical, because some concepts – like the concept of heat and cold and their likes – and some assents – like the assent that negation and affirmation do not coexist and are not both removed, and that the whole is greater than the part and their likes – are obtained without reflection and acquisition (Sharif).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection about infinite regress in acquiring knowledge. The author concedes that if by 'dependence' it means having all infinite premises present in infinite time, that is granted. However, the impossibility of infinite items in infinite time is denied unless the soul is originated. If the soul is eternal, infinite knowledge is possible in infinite time. The author notes that the objection assumes the soul is originated, which is proven elsewhere. Further, the soul can have infinite matters in summary at once; only detailed apprehension at once is impossible. Thus, infinite detailed knowledge can unfold over time. The author then refutes a potential misunderstanding: when seeking a conclusion, the inquirer needs only proximate premises, not all distant ones. The distant premises should have been acquired earlier. Finally, the author asserts that not all concepts and assents are theoretical; some are intuitive, like heat/cold and the principle of non-contradiction. This guards against the misconception that all knowledge requires infinite regress.

Key Terms
النفس
تسلسل
قوة قريبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not impossible for the soul to have infinite knowledge?
A) Because the soul is originated and finite.
B) Because the soul is eternal and can apprehend infinite matters in summary.
C) Because all knowledge is intuitive.
D) Because knowledge is acquired through senses only.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress depends on the assumption that the soul is originated; if the soul is eternal, infinite knowledge in infinite time is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument to defend the possibility of infinite reward and punishment in the afterlife based on the eternal nature of the soul. Use the same structure as the author.
?
Identify the hidden premise in a mutakallim's argument that infinite regress is impossible in causal chains, and show how the author's dialectical move can be applied.

Summary vs Detailed Knowledge and Intuitive Foundations
Author explains summary vs detailed knowledge and the existence of intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between summary (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili) knowledge and identify examples of intuitive (badihi) knowledge that do not require a chain of reasoning.

Core Idea
Some knowledge is immediate and intuitive; not every concept needs a definition or proof.

In the previous lesson, we saw that infinite knowledge is possible if the soul is eternal. But how can infinite matters be present at once? The author answers: in summary form. Moreover, he asserts that not all knowledge is theoretical; some is intuitive. This lesson unpacks these key epistemological distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Define summary (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili) knowledge
Summary knowledge is having the general idea without the particulars; detailed knowledge is having each particular distinctly. The author says infinite matters can be held in summary at once, but not in detail.
Example: knowing the entire Qur'an in summary vs knowing each verse by heart in order.
2
Explain the role of proximate potentiality
The author mentions that matters are present 'in proximate potentiality' (quwwa qariba). This means the soul is ready to derive detailed knowledge when needed, without having it all actualized.
This is like having a book in memory; you can recall details when needed.
3
Identify examples of intuitive (badihi) knowledge
The author gives examples: concepts like heat and cold, and assents like the law of non-contradiction and whole greater than part. These are known without reflection.
In usul al-fiqh, such knowledge is considered necessarily known (daruri).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عنيتم به أنه يتوقف على استحضارها في أزمنة غير متناهية فمسلّم، ولكن لانسلّم أن استحضار الأمور الغير المتناهية في الأزمنة الغير المتناهية محال ؛ وإنما يستحيل ذلك لو كانت النفس حادثة، فأما إذا كانت قديمة تكون موجودة في أزمنة غير متناهية، فجاز أن يحصل لها علوم غير متناهية في أزمنة غير متناهية. فنقول : هذا الدليل مبني على حدوث النفس، وقد برهن عليه في فن الحكمة .) - أي بالفعل - لكنّها يجب أن تجامعه مجملة - إي بالقوة القريبة – كما ذكرت في المسائل الهندسية. قلت : إدراك النفس دفعة لأمور غير متناهية مجملة غير محال، وإنما المحال إدراكها إياها دفعة مفصلة، فيجوز أن يحصل للنفس أمور غير متناهية مفصلة في أزمنة غير متناهية، وتكون تلك الأمور حاصلة لها الآن – أي عند حصول المطلوب المتوقف عليها - مجملة؛ على أنا نقول : كما جاز أن لا تكون تلك الأمور حاصلة بالفعل عند حصول المطلوب جاز أيضاً أن لا تكون حاصلة بالقوة القريبة، فلابد لنفي هذا الجواز من دليل (شريف). If you mean that it depends on their being present in infinite times, that is conceded, but we do not concede that the presence of infinite matters in infinite times is impossible; rather that would be impossible if the soul were originated, but if it is eternal and exists in infinite times, then it is possible for it to acquire infinite sciences in infinite times.
