Critique of Treating Judgment as a Concept in Tasdiq
Criticizing the view that hukm is a tasawwur in tasdiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem with considering judgment (hukm) as a conceptualization (tasawwur) in the definition of assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Judgment is an act of assertion, not a concept; conflating them misrepresents the structure of knowledge.

In logic, the distinction between simple concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) is foundational. Some scholars have attempted to define tasdiq as requiring only two concepts – the subject and predicate – but the commentator here critiques a position that would treat the judgment itself as a third concept. This lesson unpacks that critique and shows why judgment cannot be reduced to a concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim under critique
The passage begins by quoting a claim: 'And in tasdiq, judgment is necessary' and notes that it does not follow that judgment is a conceptualization. Recognize that the target is the view that hukm must be a tasawwur.
The original claim likely intended that tasdiq requires two concepts and a judgment.
2
Trace the first logical objection
The commentator argues: if 'judgment' were conjoined to 'conceptualization of the subject', and judgment is not a conceptualization, then the author would have to say something different. The key point: if hukm were a tasawwur, then tasdiq would require three elements (two concepts + judgment), contradicting the original claim that it only requires two concepts. This is the central flaw.
3
Analyse the second objection: superfluity
The commentator further argues that if judgment is not a concept, then mentioning it in the definition of tasdiq is superfluous. The goal was to show that conceptualization naturally precedes assent; judgment, being non-conceptual, has no role in establishing that precedence.
This strengthens the critique by showing the mention of hukm is irrelevant to the intended proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
تصوراً، كأنه قال : ولابد في التصديق من الحكم» وغير لازم منه أن يكون تصوّراً - وأن يكون معطوفاً على المحكوم عليه، فحينئذ يكون تصوراً.
وفيه نظر لأن قوله : «والحكم» لوكان معطوفاً على تصوّر المحكوم عليه، ولا يكون الحكم تصوّراً : لوجب أن يقول : «لامتناع الحكم تمن جهل أحد هذين الأمرين»؛ ولو صح حمل قوله : «أحد هذه الأمور» على هذا لظهر الفساد من وجه آخر ، وهو أن اللازم من ذلك استدعاء التصديق تصوّر المحكوم عليه ويه والمدعى استدعاء التصديق التصورين والحكم، فلا يكون الدليل وارداً على المدعى.
وأيضا ذكر الحكم يكون حينئذ مستدركاً، إذا المطلوب بيان تقدم التصور على التصديق طبعاً، والحكم إذا لم يكن تصوراً لم يكن له دخل في ذلك.
Commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: 'as a conceptualization, as if he said: "And in tasdiq, judgment is necessary" and it is not necessary that it be a conceptualization, and that it be conjoined to the subject of judgment, then it would be a conceptualization.' And there is a problem with this, because his statement 'and the judgment' if it were conjoined to the conceptualization of the subject of judgment, and the judgment is not a conceptualization, then he would have to say: 'because it is impossible for judgment [to occur] when one is ignorant of one of these two matters'; and if the interpretation of his statement 'one of these matters' were correct, then corruption would appear from another aspect, which is that the necessary consequence of that is that tasdiq requires the conceptualization of the subject of judgment and [??] and the claim requires tasdiq of both conceptualizations and judgment, so the evidence would not apply to the claim. Also, the mention of judgment would then be superfluous, since the desired is to show that conceptualization naturally precedes tasdiq, and judgment, if it is not a conceptualization, has no role in that.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a previous interpretation of the relationship between tasawwur and tasdiq. The commentator begins by quoting a claim: "And in tasdiq, judgment is necessary" and then notes that it does not follow that judgment itself is a tasawwur (conceptualization). The criticism proceeds: if 'judgment' were conjoined to the conceptualization of the subject, and judgment is not a conceptualization, then the author of the original statement would have had to say something like 'because judgment is impossible when one is ignorant of one of these two matters' (referring to the subject and predicate conceptualizations). But this leads to a contradiction, as it would imply that tasdiq requires both conceptualizations and judgment, yet the original claim aimed to show that tasdiq only requires two conceptualizations (subject and predicate). Thus the evidence fails. Furthermore, mentioning judgment would be superfluous because the point is to show that conceptualization precedes tasdiq by nature; judgment, if it is not a concept, has no bearing on that precedence. An Islamic science example: In knowing the ruling 'Zakat is obligatory', one must have concepts of 'zakat' and 'obligatory', but the judgment itself is an act of assent, not a concept. The confusion arises when one treats the judgment as just another concept.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the critique, what is the problem with claiming that hukm (judgment) is a tasawwur?
A) It makes tasdiq require three things instead of two.
B) It contradicts the definition of tasawwur as a simple concept.
C) It would make tasdiq impossible.
D) It was not the original author's intention.

Key Takeaway
The critique shows that judgment is an act of affirmation/denial, not a simple concept, and thus cannot be reduced to a tasawwur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'Salah is mandatory (wajib)'. Identify the concepts (tasawwurat) and the judgment (hukm). Explain why the judgment itself is not a concept.
?
In the debate over the definition of tasdiq, a scholar argues that tasdiq consists of two concepts: subject and predicate. How would you use the critique from this lesson to challenge that claim?

The Fivefold Division of the Universal by External Existence
Classification of the universal by external existence into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five categories of universal concepts by external existence, using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Universals are classified not by existence but by possibility of external instantiation.

In this passage, the author presents a fivefold classification of universal concepts based on their relationship to external existence. This classification helps us see that a universal's universality does not depend on whether it actually exists, but on whether it can be conceived as applying to multiple beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
The basis is external existence: whether the universal may exist outside the mind or not. Note that this is independent of the definition of universality.
2
List the five categories from the matn
The author states there are five: (1) impossible to exist, (2) possible but non-existent, (3) existent as one with impossibility of another, (4) existent as one with possibility of another, (5) existent as many (finite or infinite). Write them down.
The matn explicitly numbers them as five, though the commentary later expands to six.
3
Match each category with its example
For each category, find the example given: (1) partner of Allah, (2) phoenix, (3) Allah Himself, (4) sun, (5) seven planets or rational souls. Understand why each fits.
4
Understand the difference between the five and six divisions
The commentary reorganizes the classification into six by separating the 'non-multiple' category into impossible to replicate and possible to replicate, making six. This refines the schema.
The six-fold division is: impossible, possible non-existent, existent as one impossible to replicate, existent as one possible to replicate, existent as many finite, existent as many infinite.
5
Apply the classification to a new concept
Take the universal 'angel' (Jibril). In Islamic belief, there are many angels, so it would be existent as many. Is it finite? Possibly finite according to some. Classify it using the schema.

مباحث الكلي والجزئي
[٢٦] قال :
الفصل الثالث
في مباحث الكلّي والجزئي)
ممتنع الوجود وممكن الوجود وأقسامه]
وهي
خمسة:
الأول: الكلّي قد يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج لا لنفس مفهوم اللفظ
كشريك الباري عز اسمه.
وقد يكون ممكن الوجود لكن لا يوجد، كالعنقاء.
وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً فقط مع امتناع غيره – كالباري عز اسمه
أو مع إمكانه – كالشمس.
وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيراً: إما متناهياً كالكواكب السبعة السيارة، أو غير متناه كالنفوس الناطقة عند بعضهم.
1) ذكر الجزئي هاهنا على سبيل التبعية، إذ قد سبق أن ليس لصاحب هذا الفن غرض متعلّق بالجزئيات، فلا بحث له عن أحوال الجزئي، لكنه تصور مفهوميه - أعني الحقيقي الذي مضى والإضافي الذي سنذكره - وبين النسبة بين مفهوميه تتميماً للتصوير، وما يبين النسبة بين الإضافي والكلّي أيضاً توضيحاً لتصويره (شریف).

كرم الرسالة الشمسية
أقول : قد عرفت في أوّل الفصل الثاني أن ما حصل في العقل فهو من حيث أنه حاصل في العقل إن لم يكن مانعاً من اشتراكه بين كثيرين فهو الكلّيّ، وإن كان مانعاً من الاشتراك فهو الجزئي، فمناط الكلية والجزئية إنما هو الوجود العقلي، وأما كون الكلّي ممتنع الوجود في الخارج أو ممكن الوجود فيه فأمر خارج عن مفهومه.
و إلى هذا أشار بقوله : «والكلّي قد يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج لالنفس مفهوم اللفظ» يعني امتناع وجود الكلي أو إمكان وجوده شيء لا يقتضيه نفس مفهوم الكلّي، بل إذا جرد العقل النظر إليه احتمل عنده أن يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وأن يكون ممكن الوجود فيه. فالكلّي إذا نسبناه إلى الوجود الخارجي : إما أن يكون ممكن الوجود في الخارج أو ممتنع الوجود فيه ، الثاني كشريك الباري عز اسمه.
والأوّل إما أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج أو لا، الثاني كالعنقاء. والأوّل إما أن يكون متعدّد الأفراد في الخارج أولا يكون متعدد
(1) هذا الإمكان هو الإمكان العام مقيّداً بجانب الوجود، فيقابل الممتنع كما ذكره ويتناول الواجب كما سيذكره - أعني قوله « والأول كالباري» فلايتجه أن يقال : إن أراد بالإمكان الإمكان العام كان متناولاً للممتنع لا مقابلاً له، وإن أراد الإمكان الخاص فلا يندرج تحته الواجب. والحاصل أن الكلي إما معدوم في الخارج - وهو قسمان : ممتنع الوجود فيه وممكن الوجود فيه - وإما موجود في الخارج غير متعدد الأفراد " وهو أيضاً قسمان - وإما موجود متعدد الأفراد - وهو أيضاً قسمان - فانحصر أقسام الكلي
في ستة (شريف).
Discussions of the Universal and the Particular. [26] He said: Chapter Three: On the discussions of the universal and the particular. The impossible of existence and the possible of existence and their divisions. They are five: First: The universal may be impossible of existence in the external world, not because of the concept itself, like the partner of Allah, exalted is His name. And it may be possible of existence but does not exist, like the phoenix. And what exists of it may be only one with the impossibility of another – like Allah, exalted is His name, or with its possibility – like the sun. And what exists of it may be many: either finite, like the seven wandering planets, or infinite, like rational souls according to some. (1) He mentions the particular here incidentally, for it has preceded that the practitioner of this art has no purpose related to particulars; thus there is no investigation of the states of the particular. However, he conceptualizes its two meanings – the real one which has passed and the relative one which we will mention – and explains the relationship between its two meanings to complete the conceptualization. And what explains the relationship between the relative [particular] and the universal also clarifies its conceptualization (Sharif). The gift of the Shamsiyya. I say: You have known at the beginning of the second chapter that what is obtained in the intellect, inasmuch as it is obtained in the intellect, if there is no impediment to its sharing among many, then it is the universal; and if there is an impediment to sharing, then it is the particular. So the criterion for universality and particularity is only mental existence. As for the universal being impossible of existence in the external world or possible of existence therein, that is extraneous to its concept. And to this he alluded with his statement, “The universal may be impossible of existence in the external world, not because of the concept itself,” meaning impossibility of existence or possibility of existence of the universal is something not necessitated by the concept of the universal itself. Rather, if the intellect strips the examination of it, it is conceivable to it that it could be impossible of existence in the external world and that it could be possible of existence therein. So the universal, when we relate it to external existence: either it is possible of existence in the external world or impossible of existence therein. The second is like the partner of Allah, exalted is His name. The first either exists in the external world or not. The second is like the phoenix. And the first either has multiple individuals in the external world or does not have multiple individuals. (1) This possibility is general possibility restricted to the side of existence; thus it contrasts with the impossible as he mentioned and includes the necessary as he will mention – namely his statement, “The first is like Allah.” So it cannot be objected that if he meant by possibility general possibility, it would include the impossible and not contrast with it; and if he meant specific possibility, then the necessary would not be included under it. The summary is that the universal is either non-existent in the external world – and it is two divisions: impossible of existence therein and possible of existence therein – or existent in the external world without multiple individuals – and that is also two divisions – or existent with multiple individuals – and that is also two divisions. So the divisions of the universal are confined to six (Sharif).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the universal (al-kulli) in relation to its existence or non-existence in the external world, followed by a commentary that clarifies the basis of universality. The matn (original text) divides the universal into five categories based on external existence: (1) impossible in existence, like the concept of a partner to Allah (which is impossible by revelation and reason); (2) possible in existence but not actually existing, like a phoenix (a mythical bird); (3) existing as a single individual with the impossibility of another of its kind, like Allah Himself (Who is One and unique); (4) existing as a single individual with the possibility of another, like the sun (there is only one sun but another could possibly exist); (5) existing with many individuals, either finite (like the seven classical planets) or infinite (according to some, like rational souls). The commentary then re-emphasizes that universality is determined by mental existence: if a concept in the mind permits sharing among multiple particulars, it is universal; if not, it is particular. The external existence or non-existence of the universal is not part of its definition; it is a separate consideration. The commentary further reorganizes the classification into a six-fold division: the universal relative to external existence is either impossible (like partner of Allah) or possible; the possible is either non-existent (like phoenix) or existent; the existent is either non-multiple (single individual) which could be either impossible to replicate (Allah) or possible (sun) or multiple (finite or infinite). The footnote clarifies that the 'possibility' here is the general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) restricted to the side of existence, so it includes the necessary (wajib) and contrasts with the impossible, thus avoiding objections about specific possibility excluding the necessary.

Key Terms
الكلي
ممتنع الوجود
ممكن الوجود
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص

A) Partner of Allah
B) Phoenix (al-'anqa')
C) The sun
D) The seven planets

Key Takeaway
The fivefold classification demonstrates that the external existence of a universal is incidental to its nature as a universal.

?

The Criterion of Universality: Mental Existence
Commentary clarifying the basis of universality as mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the criterion for universality is mental existence, not external existence, and identify consequences for understanding logical concepts.

Core Idea
Universality is a mental attribute; external existence is irrelevant.

The commentary in this passage strongly emphasizes that the universality of a concept depends solely on its existence in the mind. A concept is universal if and only if the mind can apply it to multiple subjects without obstacle. This refutes the common misunderstanding that 'universal' implies an external reality existing outside the mind.

Guided Path
1
Locate the criterion statement
Find the sentence in the commentary that states: 'فمناط الكلية والجزئية إنما هو الوجود العقلي' (the criterion for universality and particularity is only mental existence). Write it down.
2
Paraphrase the criterion
If a concept in the intellect does not prevent its sharing among many, it is universal; if it does prevent sharing, it is particular. This is a function of the mind, not of external reality.
3
Distinguish mental existence from external existence
The commentary adds: 'وأما كون الكلّي ممتنع الوجود في الخارج أو ممكن الوجود فيه فأمر خارج عن مفهومه' (yet the universal's being impossible or possible in external existence is extraneous to its concept). Thus external existence is not part of the definition.
4
Apply the criterion to an example
Take the concept 'partner of Allah'. In the mind, this concept can be considered universally (it applies to any alleged partner), though externally it is impossible. According to the criterion, it is still a universal. Explain why.
5
Reflect on implications for Islamic theology
This criterion protects against the error of thinking that universals must be 'platonically real' or that Allah's attributes (which are universals) are separate entities. They are real in the mind and in reality but not independent substances.

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for universality according to the commentary?
A) The concept must exist externally.
B) The concept must be possible in existence.
C) The concept must not have an impediment to sharing among many in the intellect.
D) The concept must be a single individual in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Universality is defined by the mind's capacity for multiple application, not by external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the concept 'impossible partner of Allah' is considered a universal according to the criterion, despite being impossible in external existence.
?
A student argues that 'human' is not a universal because there is no external 'humanity' separate from individuals. Show how the mental criterion refutes this argument.

Relative Particular Broader Than Real Particular: The Core Claim
Commentary explains the relationship between relative and real particular with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why every real particular is a relative particular using the example of Zayd and the human essence.

Core Idea
Relative particular is broader: every real particular is relative, but not vice versa.

This passage establishes a key relationship between two types of particulars. Understanding this hierarchy is fundamental for correct definition in logic and for applying concepts in Islamic sciences. We will walk through the proof using the example of Zayd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of particular
The passage defines al-juz'i al-idafi (relative particular) and al-juz'i al-haqiqi (real particular). Note that the relative particular is particular only in relation to a broader universal, while the real particular is absolutely individual.
Distinguish carefully: real particular cannot be predicated of many; relative particular can be universal in another relation.
2
Understand the relationship stated
The claim: relative particular is more general (a'amm) than real particular, meaning every real particular is a relative particular, but not every relative particular is real.
3
Examine the proof using Zayd
Take Zayd, a real particular. Strip away his individuators (e.g., time, place, appearance). The remaining essence 'human' is universal and more general than Zayd. Thus Zayd is subsumed under a universal, making him a relative particular.
This shows that any real particular can be considered relative when its essence is abstracted.
4
Generalize the proof
The same reasoning applies to any real particular: by removing individuators, a universal essence remains, so every real particular is relative.
This is the key implication: real particular implies relative particular.
5
Note the converse is false
A relative particular can be universal in another relation (e.g., 'human' is universal relative to Zayd but particular relative to 'animal'). Therefore not every relative particular is a real particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو - أي الجزئي الإضافي - أعم من الجزئي الحقيقي، يعني أن كل جزئي حقيقي جزئي إضافي بدون العكس. أما الأوّل فلأن كل جزئي حقيقي فهو مندرج تحت ماهيته المعراة عن المشخصات، كما إذا جردنا زيداً عن المشخصات التي بها صـار شخصاً معيناً بقيت الماهية الإنسانية وهي أعم منه، فيكون كل جزئي حقيقي مندرجاً تحت أعم، فيكون جزئياً إضافياً. (۱) وهذا منقوض بواجب الوجود فإنّه شخص معين ويمتنع أن يكون له ماهيّة كلّيّة، وإلا فهو إن كان مجرد تلك الماهية الكلية يلزم أن يكون أمر واحد كلّيّاً وجزئيّاً وهو محال ، وإن كانت تلك الماهية مع شيء آخر يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود معروضاً للتشخص، وهو محال، لما تقرر في فن الحكمة أن تشخص واجب الوجود عينه. (۱) أي بذاته المخصوصة المقدسة، لا بمفهومه، فإنه كلّي كما مرّ. وأجيب عن هذا النقض بأن مناط الكلية والجزئية هو الوجود الذهني - كما صرح به - وليس من شأن الموجود المعين الذي هو الواجب الوجود لذاته أن يحصل في الذهن حتى يتصف بالجزئية ؛ بل لا يعقل إلا بوجوه تفرض كلية منحصرة في شخص. و ردّ بأن معنى الجزئي هو ما كان بحيث لو حصل في الذهن لمنع، وهذا معنى قولهم : «كل مفهوم إما أن يمنع ... الخ » إذ لم يريدوا به كونه مفهوماً بالفعل، وذلك لا يتوقف على الحصول بالفعل في الذهن ولا على إمكان حصوله فيه ؛ والجزئي الحقيقي بهذا المعنى يصدق على الواجب كما لا يخفي. وأيضاً الممتنع الحصول في الذهن هو كنه ذاته، لاذاته على وجه مخصوص تعرض له الجزئية (شريف). أما الثاني : فلجواز أن يكون الجزئي الإضافي كلّيّاً، لأنه الأخص من شيء، والأخص من شيء يجوز أن يكون كلياً تحت كلّي آخر، بخلاف الجزئي الحقيقي فإنه يمتنع أن يكون كليا.
And it - i.e., the relative particular - is more general than the real particular, meaning that every real particular is a relative particular without the converse. As for the first, because every real particular is subsumed under its essence stripped of individuators; just as if we strip Zayd of the individuators by which he became a specific person, the human essence remains and it is more general than him, so every real particular is subsumed under something more general, thus it is a relative particular. (1) This is refuted by the Necessary Being, for He is a specific person and it is impossible for Him to have a universal essence; otherwise, if He were merely that universal essence, it would necessitate that one thing be both universal and particular, which is absurd; and if that essence were with something else, it would necessitate that the Necessary Being be subject to individuation, which is absurd, because it is established in the field of philosophy that the individuation of the Necessary Being is His essence. (1) Meaning by His own specific holy essence, not by His concept, for that is universal as mentioned. And this refutation was answered by saying that the criterion of universality and particularity is mental existence - as stated - and it is not in the nature of the specific existent which is the Necessary Being in Himself to occur in the mind so that it may be described as particular; rather, it is not conceptualized except through aspects that are assumed to be universal confined to a person. And it was rebutted that the meaning of particular is that which, if it were to occur in the mind, would prevent [predication], and this is the meaning of their saying: 'Every concept either prevents... etc.' for they did not intend it to be an actual concept, and that does not depend on its actual occurrence in the mind nor on the possibility of its occurrence therein; and the real particular in this sense applies to the Necessary Being, as is not hidden. Moreover, that which is impossible to occur in the mind is the quintessence of His essence, not His essence in a specific aspect to which particularity attaches (Sharif). As for the second: because it is possible for the relative particular to be universal, since it is more specific than something, and what is more specific than something can be universal under another universal, unlike the real particular, for it is impossible for it to be universal.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definition of the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) and its relationship to the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi). The author begins by stating that the relative particular is more general than the real particular: every real particular is a relative particular, but not vice versa. The first point is proved by considering a real particular like Zayd: when stripped of its individuators, the universal human essence remains, which is more general, thus Zayd is subsumed under a universal and is therefore a relative particular. Then an objection (naqd) is raised concerning the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud): He is a specific person (real particular) but has no universal essence, so He cannot be a relative particular, contradicting the initial claim. The objection argues that if He had a universal essence, He would be both universal and particular, or would be subject to individuation, both of which are impossible. A gloss clarifies that the objection concerns His essence, not His concept. The answer (jawab) first argues that the criterion for particularity and universality is mental existence; the Necessary Being, being a concrete external existent, cannot occur in the mind to be described as particular. This answer is then rebutted: the definition of a particular is based on the potential to prevent predication if it were in the mind, not on actual mental existence. In this sense, the Necessary Being qualifies as a real particular. The rebuttal adds that what is impossible to obtain in the mind is only the essence of the Necessary Being, not the aspect in which particularity inheres (as per a marginal note by Sharif). Finally, the second point is made: the relative particular can be universal because it is more specific than something, and that more specific thing can be a universal under another universal; the real particular cannot be universal. This completes the discussion of the relative and real particular.

الجزئي الحقيقي
مناط الكلية والجزئية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true?
A) Every relative particular is a real particular.
B) Every real particular is a relative particular.
C) The Necessary Being is a relative particular.
D) The relative particular is more specific than the real particular.

Key Takeaway
Every real particular is a relative particular because it can be subsumed under a universal essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a real particular like 'this specific prayer of Fajr performed by Zayd'. Identify its universal essence by stripping individuators (time, place, person). Show that it becomes a relative particular under the universal 'salah'.
?

The Objection of the Necessary Being and the Criterion of Particularity
Commentary addresses a dialectical objection about the Necessary Being and the criterion of particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the objection about the Necessary Being and evaluate the responses regarding the criterion of particularity.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being challenges the claim that every particular is relative, but the criterion of particularity is based on potential mental existence.

The commentator anticipates a powerful objection: the Necessary Being is a real particular but cannot be subsumed under any universal essence. This leads to a deep discussion about what makes something particular. We will trace the dialectical exchange and assess the arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the objection (naqd)
The objector argues: The Necessary Being (God) is a specific person (real particular) but has no universal essence. Therefore He is not a relative particular, contradicting the claim that every real particular is relative.
This is a strong objection because if God had a universal essence, He would be both universal and particular, or would be subject to individuation, both absurd.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the objection
If the Necessary Being had a universal essence, either He is just that essence (then one thing is both universal and particular) or He is essence plus something else (individuator), which would make Him subject to external individuation, contradicting the established fact that His individuation is His essence.
3
Examine the first answer (jawab)
The answer: The criterion of universality and particularity is mental existence. The Necessary Being, as a concrete external existent, cannot occur in the mind, so He cannot be described as particular. Therefore He does not fall under the claim.
This answer appeals to the distinction between mental and external existence.
4
Analyse the rebuttal (radd)
The rebuttal: The definition of particular is based on the potential to prevent predication if it were in the mind, not on actual mental existence. In that hypothetical sense, the Necessary Being is a real particular because if His essence were conceived, it would prevent predication of many. Thus the first answer fails.
5
Note the marginal clarification by Sharif
Sharif notes that what is impossible to occur in the mind is the quintessence of the Necessary Being, not His essence in the aspect that particularity attaches to. So the concept of particularity can still apply.
This refinement preserves the initial claim that every real particular is relative, while accommodating the unique status of the Necessary Being.
